Phillips</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Rupert_Read" title="Rupert Read">Rupert Read</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Barry_Stroud" title="Barry Stroud">Barry Stroud</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Toulmin" title="Stephen Toulmin">Stephen Toulmin</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Michael_Williams_(philosopher)" title="Michael Williams (philosopher)">Michael Williams</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Crispin_Wright" title="Crispin Wright">Crispin Wright</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:lavender">Other topics</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Apostles" title="Cambridge Apostles">Cambridge Apostles</a></li>\n<li><div style="padding:0.2em 0.4em; line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Moral_Sciences_Club" title="Cambridge University Moral Sciences Club">Cambridge University<br />Moral Sciences Club</a></div></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/New_Wittgenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="New Wittgenstein">New Wittgenstein</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Haus_Wittgenstein" title="Haus Wittgenstein">Stonborough House</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;padding-top:0;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Wittgenstein" title="Template:Wittgenstein"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Wittgenstein" title="Template talk:Wittgenstein"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Ludwig Josef Johann Wittgenstein</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;v&#39; in &#39;vie&#39;">v</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span><span title="/\xc9\x99n/: &#39;on&#39; in &#39;button&#39;">\xc9\x99n</span><span title="/\xca\x83/: &#39;sh&#39; in &#39;shy&#39;">\xca\x83</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/a\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">a\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>, <wbr />-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/a\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">a\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <small>German:&#32;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[\xcb\x88v\xc9\xaatg\xc9\x99n\xcb\x8c\xca\x83ta\xc9\xaan]</a></span>; 26 April 1889 \xe2\x80\x93 29 April 1951) was an Austrian-British philosopher who worked primarily in <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics" title="Philosophy of mathematics">philosophy of mathematics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">philosophy of language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Time_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Time-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> From 1929 to 1947, Wittgenstein taught at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> During his lifetime he published just one slim book, the 75-page <i><a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Logico-Philosophicus" title="Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus">Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus</a></i> (1921),  one article, one book review and a children\'s dictionary.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="#Legacy">His voluminous manuscripts</a> were edited and published posthumously.  <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Investigations" title="Philosophical Investigations">Philosophical Investigations</a></i> appeared as a book in 1953, and has since come to be recognised as one of the most important works of philosophy in the twentieth century.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> His teacher, <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>, described Wittgenstein as "the most perfect example I have ever known of genius as traditionally conceived; passionate, profound, intense, and dominating".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988118_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988118-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a> into one of Europe\'s richest families, he inherited a fortune from his father in 1913. He initially made some donations to artists and writers and then, in a period of severe personal depression after the First World War, he gave away his entire fortune to his brothers and sisters.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Three of his brothers committed <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a>, with Wittgenstein contemplating it too.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> He left academia several times\xe2\x80\x94serving as an officer on the front line during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, where he was <a href="/wiki/Military_awards_and_decorations" title="Military awards and decorations">decorated</a> a number of times for his courage; teaching in schools in remote Austrian villages where he encountered controversy for hitting children when they made mistakes in mathematics; and working as a <a href="/wiki/Hospital_porter" class="mw-redirect" title="Hospital porter">hospital porter</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in London where he told patients not to take the drugs they were prescribed while largely managing to keep secret the fact that he was one of the world\'s most famous philosophers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990232\xe2\x80\x93233,_431_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990232\xe2\x80\x93233,_431-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> He described philosophy as "the only work that gives me real satisfaction".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>His philosophy is often divided into an early period, exemplified by the <i>Tractatus</i>, and a later period, articulated in the <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>. The early Wittgenstein was concerned with the logical relationship between propositions and the world and believed that by providing an account of the logic underlying this relationship, he had solved all philosophical problems. The later Wittgenstein rejected many of the assumptions of the <i>Tractatus</i>, arguing that the meaning of words is best understood as their use within a given <a href="/wiki/Language-game" class="mw-redirect" title="Language-game">language-game</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A survey among American university and college teachers ranked the <i>Investigations</i> as the most important book of 20th-century philosophy, standing out as "the one crossover masterpiece in twentieth-century philosophy, appealing across diverse specializations and philosophical orientations."<sup id="cite_ref-here_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-here-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Investigations</i> also ranked 54th on a list of most influential twentieth-century works in <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a> prepared by the University of Minnesota\'s Center for Cognitive Sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-milproj_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-milproj-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> However, in the words of his friend <a href="/wiki/Georg_Henrik_von_Wright" title="Georg Henrik von Wright">Georg Henrik von Wright</a>, he believed "his ideas were generally misunderstood and distorted even by those who professed to be his disciples. He doubted he would be better understood in the future. He once said he felt as though he was writing for people who would think in a different way, breathe a different air of life, from that of present-day men."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" background and Hitler</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#1906\xe2\x80\x931913:_University"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">1906\xe2\x80\x931913: University</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Engineering_at_Berlin_and_Manchester"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Engineering at Berlin and Manchester</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Arrival_at_Cambridge"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Cambridge Moral Sciences Club and Apostles</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Frustrations_at_Cambridge/Pre-war"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span href="#1941\xe2\x80\x931947:_World_War_II_and_Guy&#39;s_Hospital"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">1941\xe2\x80\x931947: World War II and Guy\'s Hospital</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#1947\xe2\x80\x931951:_Final_years"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">1947\xe2\x80\x931951: Final years</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#1953:_Publication_of_the_Philosophical_Investigations"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">1953: Publication of the <i>Philosophical Investigations</i></span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Interpreters"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Interpreters</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-38"><a href="#Bertrand_Russell"><span class="tocnumber">10.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Bertrand Russell</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-39"><a href="#Saul_Kripke"><span class="tocnumber">10.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Saul Kripke</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Works"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Works</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-41"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">13.1</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">13.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#Bergen_and_Cambridge_archives"><span class="tocnumber">14.1</span> <span class="toctext">Bergen and Cambridge archives</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Papers_about_his_Nachlass"><span class="tocnumber">14.2</span> <span class="toctext">Papers about his <i>Nachlass</i></span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Other"><span class="tocnumber">14.3</span> <span class="toctext">Other</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-49"><a href="#Works_referencing_Wittgenstein"><span class="tocnumber">14.4</span> <span class="toctext">Works referencing Wittgenstein</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Wittgensteins">The Wittgensteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The Wittgensteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Karl_Wittgenstein" title="Karl Wittgenstein">Karl Wittgenstein</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg/150px-Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg" width="150" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="177" data-file-height="277" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Karl_Wittgenstein.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Karl_Wittgenstein" title="Karl Wittgenstein">Karl Wittgenstein</a> was one of the richest men in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Bramann_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bramann-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>According to a family tree prepared in Jerusalem after World War II, Wittgenstein\'s paternal great-great-grandfather was Moses Meier,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Ashkenazi_Jews" title="Ashkenazi Jews">Jewish</a> land agent who lived with his wife, Brendel Simon, in <a href="/wiki/Bad_Laasphe" title="Bad Laasphe">Bad Laasphe</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Siegen-Wittgenstein" title="Siegen-Wittgenstein">Principality of Wittgenstein</a>, Westphalia.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In July 1808, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> issued a decree that everyone, including Jews, must adopt an inheritable <a href="/wiki/Jewish_surname" title="Jewish surname">family surname</a>, and so Meier\'s son, also Moses, took the name of his employers, the <a href="/wiki/Sayn-Wittgenstein" title="Sayn-Wittgenstein">Sayn-Wittgensteins</a>, and became Moses Meier Wittgenstein.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> His son, Hermann Christian Wittgenstein\xe2\x80\x94who took the middle name "Christian" to distance himself from his Jewish background\xe2\x80\x94married Fanny Figdor, also Jewish, who converted to <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> just before they married, and the couple founded a successful business trading in wool in Leipzig.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk19904-5_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk19904-5-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Ludwig\'s grandmother Fanny was a first cousin of the famous violinist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Joachim" title="Joseph Joachim">Joseph Joachim</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk19905_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk19905-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>They had 11 children\xe2\x80\x94among them Wittgenstein\'s father. Karl Otto Clemens Wittgenstein (1847\xe2\x80\x931913) became an industrial tycoon, and by the late 1880s was one of the richest men in Europe, with an effective monopoly on Austria\'s steel cartel.<sup id="cite_ref-Bramann_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bramann-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated63_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated63-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Thanks to Karl, the Wittgensteins became the second wealthiest family in <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>, only behind the <a href="/wiki/Rothschild_family" title="Rothschild family">Rothschilds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated63_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated63-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> As a result of his decision in 1898 to invest substantially in the Netherlands and in Switzerland as well as overseas, particularly in the US, the family was to an extent shielded from the hyperinflation that hit Austria in 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk19907_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk19907-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> However, their wealth diminished due to post-1918 hyperinflation and subsequently during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>, although even as late as 1938 they owned 13 mansions in Vienna alone.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wittgenstein" title="Paul Wittgenstein">Paul Wittgenstein</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:160px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1._The_infant_Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/1._The_infant_Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg/158px-1._The_infant_Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg" width="158" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/1._The_infant_Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg/237px-1._The_infant_Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/1._The_infant_Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg/316px-1._The_infant_Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg 2x" data-file-width="474" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1._The_infant_Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ludwig, c. 1890s</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:165px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg/163px-The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg" width="163" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/37/The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg/245px-The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/37/The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg 2x" data-file-width="264" data-file-height="377" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Wittgensteins_1890.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>From left, Helene, Rudi, Hermine, Ludwig (the baby), <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Stonborough-Wittgenstein" title="Margaret Stonborough-Wittgenstein">Gretl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wittgenstein" title="Paul Wittgenstein">Paul</a>, Hans, and Kurt, around 1890</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:169px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_as_a_child.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ludwig_as_a_child.jpg/167px-Ludwig_as_a_child.jpg" width="167" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ludwig_as_a_child.jpg/251px-Ludwig_as_a_child.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Ludwig_as_a_child.jpg/334px-Ludwig_as_a_child.jpg 2x" data-file-width="748" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_as_a_child.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ludwig sitting in a field as a child</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg/220px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9f/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="295" data-file-height="195" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_siblings.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ludwig (bottom-right), Paul, and their sisters, late 1890s</div></div></div>\n<p>Wittgenstein\'s mother was Leopoldine Maria Josefa Kalmus, known among friends as Poldi. Her father was a <a href="/wiki/Czechs" title="Czechs">Bohemian</a> <a href="/wiki/Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Jew">Jew</a> and her mother was <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austrian</a>-<a href="/wiki/Slovenes" title="Slovenes">Slovene</a> <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>\xe2\x80\x94she was Wittgenstein\'s only non-Jewish grandparent.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> She was an aunt of the Nobel Prize laureate <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a> on her maternal side. Wittgenstein was born at 8:30 pm on 26 April 1889 in the so-called "Wittgenstein Palace" at Alleegasse 16, now the Argentinierstrasse, near the <a href="/wiki/Karlskirche" title="Karlskirche">Karlskirche</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Karl and Poldi had nine children in all. There were four girls: Hermine, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Stonborough-Wittgenstein" title="Margaret Stonborough-Wittgenstein">Margaret</a> (Gretl), Helene, and a fourth daughter Dora who died as a baby; and five boys: Johannes (Hans), Kurt, Rudolf (Rudi), <a href="/wiki/Paul_Wittgenstein" title="Paul Wittgenstein">Paul</a>\xe2\x80\x94who became a concert pianist despite losing an arm in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>\xe2\x80\x94and Ludwig, who was the youngest of the family.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartley16_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartley16-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The children were baptized as Catholics, received formal Catholic instruction, and were raised in an exceptionally intense environment.<sup id="cite_ref-MW_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The family was at the center of Vienna\'s cultural life; <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Walter" title="Bruno Walter">Bruno Walter</a> described the life at the Wittgensteins\' palace as an "all-pervading atmosphere of humanity and culture."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk19908_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk19908-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Karl was a leading patron of the arts, commissioning works by <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Rodin" title="Auguste Rodin">Auguste Rodin</a> and financing the city\'s exhibition hall and art gallery, the <a href="/wiki/Secession_hall_(Austria)" class="mw-redirect" title="Secession hall (Austria)">Secession Building</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Klimt" title="Gustav Klimt">Gustav Klimt</a> painted Wittgenstein\'s sister for her wedding portrait, and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Brahms" title="Johannes Brahms">Johannes Brahms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Mahler" title="Gustav Mahler">Gustav Mahler</a> gave regular concerts in the family\'s numerous music rooms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk19908_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk19908-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198818_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198818-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For Wittgenstein, who highly valued precision and discipline, <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Contemporary music">contemporary music</a> was never considered acceptable at all. "Music," he said to his friend Drury in 1930, "came to a full stop with <a href="/wiki/Brahms" class="mw-redirect" title="Brahms">Brahms</a>; and even in Brahms I can begin to hear the noise of machinery."<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Ludwig Wittgenstein himself had <a href="/wiki/Absolute_pitch" title="Absolute pitch">absolute pitch</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> and his devotion to music remained vitally important to him throughout his life: he made frequent use of musical examples and metaphors in his philosophical writings, and was unusually adept at whistling lengthy and detailed musical passages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990442-443_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990442-443-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> He also learnt to play the <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinet</a> in his thirties.<sup id="cite_ref-Monk15_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monk15-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> A fragment of music (three bars), composed by Wittgenstein, was discovered in one of his 1931 notebooks, by <a href="/wiki/Michael_Nedo" title="Michael Nedo">Michael Nedo</a>, Director of the Wittgenstein Institute in Cambridge.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Family_temperament_and_the_brothers.27_suicides"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_temperament_and_the_brothers\'_suicides">Family temperament and the brothers\' suicides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Family temperament and the brothers&#039; suicides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ray_Monk" title="Ray Monk">Ray Monk</a> writes that Karl\'s aim was to turn his sons into captains of industry; they were not sent to school lest they acquire bad habits, but were educated at home to prepare them for work in Karl\'s industrial empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199011_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199011-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Three of the five brothers would later commit suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-Kenny_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kenny-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Fitzgerald_(psychiatrist)" title="Michael Fitzgerald (psychiatrist)">Michael Fitzgerald</a> argues that Karl was a harsh perfectionist who lacked empathy, and that Wittgenstein\'s mother was anxious and insecure, unable to stand up to her husband.<sup id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Johannes Brahms said of the family, whom he visited regularly: "They seemed to act towards one another as if they were at court."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated63_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated63-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The family appeared to have a strong streak of depression running through it. <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Gottlieb" title="Anthony Gottlieb">Anthony Gottlieb</a> tells a story about Paul practicing on one of the pianos in the Wittgensteins\' main family mansion, when he suddenly shouted at Ludwig in the next room: "I cannot play when you are in the house, as I feel your scepticism seeping towards me from under the door!"<sup id="cite_ref-Gottlieb_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gottlieb-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The family Palace housed seven grand pianos<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> and each of the siblings pursued music "with an enthusiasm that, at times, bordered on the pathological."<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The eldest brother, Hans, was hailed as a musical prodigy. At the age of four, writes <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Waugh" title="Alexander Waugh">Alexander Waugh</a>, Hans could identify the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler effect</a> in a passing siren as a quarter-tone drop in pitch, and at five started crying "Wrong! Wrong!" when two brass bands in a carnival played the same tune in different <a href="/wiki/Key_signature" title="Key signature">keys</a>. But he died in mysterious circumstances in May 1902, when he ran away to America and disappeared from a boat in <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay" title="Chesapeake Bay">Chesapeake Bay</a>, most likely having committed suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Two years later, aged 22 and studying chemistry at the <a href="/wiki/Prussian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Prussian Academy of Sciences">Berlin Academy</a>, the third eldest brother, Rudi, committed suicide in a Berlin bar. He had asked the pianist to play <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Koschat" title="Thomas Koschat">Thomas Koschat</a>\'s "Verlassen, verlassen, verlassen bin ich" ("Forsaken, forsaken, forsaken am I"),<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> before mixing himself a drink of milk and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_cyanide" title="Potassium cyanide">potassium cyanide</a>. He had left several suicide notes, one to his parents that said he was grieving over the death of a friend, and another that referred to his "perverted disposition". It was reported at the time that he had sought advice from the <a href="/wiki/Scientific-Humanitarian_Committee" title="Scientific-Humanitarian Committee">Scientific-Humanitarian Committee</a>, an organization that was campaigning against <a href="/wiki/Paragraph_175" title="Paragraph 175">Paragraph 175</a> of the German Criminal Code, which prohibited homosexual sex. His father forbade the family from ever mentioning his name again.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The second eldest brother, Kurt, an officer and company director, shot himself on 27 October 1918 at the end of World War I, when the Austrian troops he was commanding refused to obey his orders and deserted <i>en masse</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199011_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199011-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> According to Gottlieb, Hermine had said Kurt seemed to carry "...the germ of disgust for life within himself."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Later Wittgenstein wrote: "I ought to have... become a star in the sky. Instead of which I have remained stuck on earth."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988156_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988156-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="1903.E2.80.931906:_Realschule_in_Linz"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1903\xe2\x80\x931906:_Realschule_in_Linz">1903\xe2\x80\x931906: Realschule in Linz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1903\xe2\x80\x931906: Realschule in Linz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Realschule_in_Linz">Realschule in Linz</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Realschule in Linz">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Realschule_in_Linz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/44/Realschule_in_Linz.jpg/170px-Realschule_in_Linz.jpg" width="170" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/44/Realschule_in_Linz.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="221" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Realschule_in_Linz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Realschule" title="Realschule">Realschule</a></i> in Linz</div></div></div>\n<p>Wittgenstein was taught by private tutors at home until he was fourteen years old. Subsequently, for three years, he attended a school. After the deaths of Hans and Rudi, Karl relented, and allowed Paul and Ludwig to be sent to school. Waugh writes that it was too late for Wittgenstein to pass his exams for the more academic <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">Gymnasium</a> in Wiener Neustadt; having had no formal schooling, he failed his entrance exam and only barely managed after extra tutoring to pass the exam for the more technically oriented <a href="/wiki/K.u.k." class="mw-redirect" title="K.u.k.">k.u.k.</a> <a href="/wiki/Realschule" title="Realschule">Realschule</a> in <a href="/wiki/Linz" title="Linz">Linz</a>, a small state school with 300 pupils.<sup id="cite_ref-Waugh33_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh33-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In 1903, when he was 14, he began his three years of formal schooling there, lodging nearby in term time with the family of Dr. Josef Strigl, a teacher at the local gymnasium, the family giving him the nickname Luki.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198851_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198851-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sandgruber_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandgruber-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On starting at the Realschule, Wittgenstein had been moved forward a year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198851_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198851-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Brigitte_Hamann" title="Brigitte Hamann">Brigitte Hamann</a> writes that he stood out from the other boys: he spoke an unusually pure form of <a href="/wiki/High_German" class="mw-redirect" title="High German">High German</a> with a stutter, dressed elegantly, and was sensitive and unsociable.<sup id="cite_ref-Hamann15_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hamann15-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Monk writes that the other boys made fun of him, singing after him: "<i>Wittgenstein wandelt wehm\xc3\xbctig widriger Winde wegen Wienw\xc3\xa4rts</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-Monk15_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monk15-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> ("Wittgenstein strolls wistfully Vienna-wards due to adverse winds"). In his leaving certificate, he received a top mark (5) in religious studies; a 2 for conduct and English, 3 for French, geography, history, mathematics and physics, and 4 for German, chemistry, geometry and freehand drawing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198851_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198851-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> He had particular difficulty with spelling and failed his written German exam because of it. He wrote in 1931: "My bad spelling in youth, up to the age of about 18 or 19, is connected with the whole of the rest of my character (my weakness in study)."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198851_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198851-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faith">Faith</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Faith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Wittgenstein was baptized as an infant by a Catholic priest and received formal instruction in Catholic doctrine as a child, as was common at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-MW_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MW-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In an interview, his sister Gretl Stonborough-Wittgenstein says that their grandfather\'s "strong, severe, partly ascetic Christianity" was a strong influence on all the Wittgenstein children.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>  It was while he was at the <i>Realschule</i> that he decided he had lost his faith in God and became an <a href="/wiki/Atheist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheist">atheist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Monk18_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monk18-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> He nevertheless believed in the importance of the idea of <a href="/wiki/Confession" title="Confession">confession</a>. He wrote in his diaries about having made a major confession to his oldest sister, Hermine, while he was at the <i>Realschule</i>; Monk speculates that it may have been about his loss of faith. He also discussed it with Gretl, his other sister, who directed him to <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_as_Will_and_Representation" title="The World as Will and Representation">The World as Will and Representation</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Monk18_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monk18-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> As a teenager, Wittgenstein adopted Schopenhauer\'s epistemological idealism. However, after his study of the philosophy of mathematics, he abandoned epistemological idealism for <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>\'s conceptual realism.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In later years, Wittgenstein was highly dismissive of Schopenhauer, describing him as an ultimately "shallow" thinker: "Schopenhauer has quite a crude mind ... where real depth starts, his comes to an end."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wittgenstein\'s religious faith and his relationship with Christianity and religion, in general (for which he professed a sincere and devoted reverence<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>) would change over time, much like his philosophical ideas. In 1912, Wittgenstein wrote to Russell saying that Mozart and Beethoven were the actual sons of God.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200834_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200834-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> However, Wittgenstein resisted formal religion, saying it was hard for him to "bend the knee",<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> though his grandfather\'s beliefs continued to influence Wittgenstein\xe2\x80\x94as he famously said, "I cannot help seeing every problem from a religious point of view".<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein referred to <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> in his <i>Philosophical Investigations.</i> Philosophically, Wittgenstein\'s thought shows fundamental alignment with religious discourse.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> For example, Wittgenstein would become one of the century\'s fiercest critics of <a href="/wiki/Scientism" title="Scientism">Scientism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With age, a deepening personal spirituality led to several elucidations and clarifications, as he untangled language problems in religion, attacking, for example, the temptation to think of God\'s existence as a matter of scientific evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In 1947, finding it more difficult to work, he wrote, "I have had a letter from an old friend in Austria, a priest. In it he says that he hopes my work will go well, if it should be God\'s will. Now that is all I want: if it should be God\'s will."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In Wittgenstein\'s <i>Culture and Value</i>, he writes, "Is what I am doing [my work in philosophy] really worth the effort? Yes, but only if a light shines on it from above." His close friend <a href="/wiki/Norman_Malcolm" title="Norman Malcolm">Norman Malcolm</a> would write, "Wittgenstein\xe2\x80\x99s mature life was strongly marked by religious thought and feeling. I am inclined to think that he was more deeply religious than are many people who correctly regard themselves as religious believers."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> At last, Wittgenstein writes, "Bach wrote on the title page of his <i><a href="/wiki/Orgelb%C3%BCchlein" title="Orgelb\xc3\xbcchlein">Orgelb\xc3\xbcchlein</a></i>, \xe2\x80\x98To the glory of the most high God, and that my neighbour may be benefited thereby.\xe2\x80\x99  That is what I would have liked to say about my work."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_of_Otto_Weininger">Influence of Otto Weininger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Influence of Otto Weininger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg/170px-OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg" width="170" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg/255px-OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg/340px-OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg 2x" data-file-width="437" data-file-height="513" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OttoWeiningerspring1903.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Austrian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Otto_Weininger" title="Otto Weininger">Otto Weininger</a> (1880\xe2\x80\x931903)</div></div></div>\n<p>While a student at the <i>Realschule</i>, Wittgenstein was influenced by Austrian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Otto_Weininger" title="Otto Weininger">Otto Weininger</a>\'s 1903 book <i>Geschlecht und Charakter</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Sex_and_Character" title="Sex and Character">Sex and Character</a></i>).\n</p><p>\nWeininger (1880\xe2\x80\x931903), who was also Jewish, argued that the concepts male and female exist only as <a href="/wiki/Platonic_realism" title="Platonic realism">Platonic forms</a>, and that Jews tend to embody the platonic femininity. Whereas men are basically rational, women operate only at the level of their emotions and sexual organs. Jews, Weininger argued, are similar, saturated with femininity, with no sense of right and wrong, and no soul. Weininger argues that man must choose between his masculine and feminine sides, consciousness and unconsciousness, Platonic love and sexuality. Love and sexual desire stand in contradiction, and love between a woman and a man is therefore doomed to misery or immorality. The only life worth living is the spiritual one\xe2\x80\x94to live as a woman or a Jew means one has no right to live at all; the choice is genius or death. Weininger committed suicide, shooting himself in 1903, shortly after publishing the book.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199019-26_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199019-26-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Many years later, as a professor at Cambridge, Wittgenstein distributed copies of Weininger\'s book to his bemused academic colleagues. He said that Weininger\'s arguments were wrong, but that it was the way they were wrong that was interesting.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In a letter dated 23 August 1931, Wittgenstein wrote the following to <a href="/wiki/G._E._Moore" title="G. E. Moore">G. E. Moore</a>;</p><blockquote><p>Dear Moore,</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>Thanks for your letter. I can quite imagine that you don\xe2\x80\x99t admire Weininger very much, what with that beastly translation and the fact that W. must feel very foreign to you. It is true that he is fantastic but he is great and fantastic. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t necessary or rather not possible to agree with him but the greatness lies in that with which we disagree. It is his enormous mistake which is great. I.e. roughly speaking if you just add a \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x88\xbc\xe2\x80\x9d to the whole book it says an important truth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness2008141_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness2008141-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>In an unusual move, Wittgenstein took out a copy of Weininger\'s work on 1 June 1931 from the Special Order Books in the university library. He met Moore on 2 June where he probably gave Moore the copy of Weininger\'s work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness2008141_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness2008141-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jewish_background_and_Hitler">Jewish background and Hitler</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Jewish background and Hitler">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Austria" title="History of the Jews in Austria">History of the Jews in Austria</a></div>\n<p>There is much debate about the extent to which Wittgenstein and his siblings, who were of 3/4 Jewish descent, saw themselves as Jews. The issue has arisen in particular regarding Wittgenstein\'s schooldays, because <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> was, for a while, at the same school at the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-McGuinnessStern_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGuinnessStern-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Goldstein" title="Laurence Goldstein">Laurence Goldstein</a> argues it is "overwhelmingly probable" the boys met each other: that Hitler would have disliked Wittgenstein, a "stammering, precocious, precious, aristocratic upstart ..."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Other commentators have dismissed as irresponsible and uninformed any suggestion that Wittgenstein\'s wealth and unusual personality may have fed Hitler\'s antisemitism, in part because there is no indication that Hitler would have seen Wittgenstein as Jewish.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wittgenstein and Hitler were born just six days apart, though Hitler had to re-sit his mathematics exam before being allowed into a higher class, while Wittgenstein was moved forward by one, so they ended up two grades apart at the <i>Realschule</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Monk estimates they were both at the school during the 1904\xe2\x80\x931905 school year, but says there is no evidence they had anything to do with each other.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Several commentators have argued that a school photograph of Hitler may show Wittgenstein in the lower left corner,<sup id="cite_ref-image_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-image-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> but Hamann says the photograph stems from 1900 or 1901, before Wittgenstein\'s time.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In his own writings<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein frequently referred to himself as Jewish, at times as part of an apparent self-flagellation. For example, while berating himself for being a "reproductive" as opposed to "productive" thinker, he attributed this to his own Jewish sense of identity, writing: "The saint is the only Jewish genius. Even the greatest Jewish thinker is no more than talented. (Myself for instance)."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> While Wittgenstein would later claim that "[m]y thoughts are 100% Hebraic,"<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> as <a href="/wiki/Hans_Sluga" title="Hans Sluga">Hans Sluga</a> has argued, if so, "His was a self-doubting Judaism, which had always the possibility of collapsing into a destructive self-hatred (as it did in <a href="/wiki/Otto_Weininger" title="Otto Weininger">Weininger\'s</a> case) but which also held an immense promise of innovation and genius."<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="1906.E2.80.931913:_University"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1906\xe2\x80\x931913:_University">1906\xe2\x80\x931913: University</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1906\xe2\x80\x931913: University">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering_at_Berlin_and_Manchester">Engineering at Berlin and Manchester</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Engineering at Berlin and Manchester">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:10._Ludwig_Wittgenstein,_aged_about_eighteen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/10._Ludwig_Wittgenstein%2C_aged_about_eighteen.jpg/220px-10._Ludwig_Wittgenstein%2C_aged_about_eighteen.jpg" width="220" height="335" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/10._Ludwig_Wittgenstein%2C_aged_about_eighteen.jpg/330px-10._Ludwig_Wittgenstein%2C_aged_about_eighteen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/10._Ludwig_Wittgenstein%2C_aged_about_eighteen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="394" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:10._Ludwig_Wittgenstein,_aged_about_eighteen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ludwig Wittgenstein, aged about eighteen</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg/220px-1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg/330px-1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg/440px-1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1720" data-file-height="1088" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1895_technische_hochschule_charlottenburg.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The old <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Institute_of_Technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Berlin Institute of Technology">Technische Hochschule</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charlottenburg" title="Charlottenburg">Charlottenburg</a>, Berlin</div></div></div>\n<p>He began his studies in mechanical engineering at the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Institute_of_Technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Berlin Institute of Technology">Technische Hochschule</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charlottenburg" title="Charlottenburg">Charlottenburg</a>, Berlin, on 23 October 1906, lodging with the family of professor Dr. Jolles. He attended for three semesters, and was awarded a <a href="/wiki/Diploma" title="Diploma">diploma</a> (<i>Abgangzeugnis</i>) on 5 May 1908.\n</p><p>During his time at the Institute, Wittgenstein developed an interest in <a href="/wiki/Aeronautics" title="Aeronautics">aeronautics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199027_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199027-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> He arrived at the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_University_of_Manchester" title="Victoria University of Manchester">Victoria University of Manchester</a> in the spring of 1908 to study for a doctorate, full of plans for aeronautical projects, including designing and flying his own plane. He conducted research into the behavior of kites in the upper atmosphere, experimenting at a meteorological observation site near <a href="/wiki/Glossop" title="Glossop">Glossop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199029_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199029-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Specifically, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Meteorological_Society" title="Royal Meteorological Society">Royal Meteorological Society</a> researched and investigated the ionization of the upper atmosphere, by suspending instruments on balloons or kites. At Glossop Wittgenstein worked under Professor of Physics Sir <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schuster" title="Arthur Schuster">Arthur Schuster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lemco_39\xe2\x80\x9351_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lemco_39\xe2\x80\x9351-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He also worked on the design of a propeller with small jet engines on the end of its blades, something he patented in 1911, and which earned him a research studentship from the university in the autumn of 1908.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199030-35_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199030-35-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> At the time, contemporary propeller designs were not advanced enough to actually put Wittgenstein\xe2\x80\x99s ideas into practice, and it would be years before a blade design that could support Wittgenstein\xe2\x80\x99s innovative design was created. Wittgenstein\xe2\x80\x99s design required air and gas to be forced along the propeller arms to combustion chambers on the end of each blade, where it was then compressed by the centrifugal force exerted by the revolving arms and ignited. Propellers of the time were typically wood, whereas modern blades are made from pressed steel laminates as separate halves, which are then welded together. This gives the blade a hollow interior, and therefore creates an ideal pathway for the air and gas.<sup id="cite_ref-Lemco_39\xe2\x80\x9351_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lemco_39\xe2\x80\x9351-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:11._With_Eccles_at_the_Kite-Flying_Station_in_Glossop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/11._With_Eccles_at_the_Kite-Flying_Station_in_Glossop.jpg/220px-11._With_Eccles_at_the_Kite-Flying_Station_in_Glossop.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/11._With_Eccles_at_the_Kite-Flying_Station_in_Glossop.jpg/330px-11._With_Eccles_at_the_Kite-Flying_Station_in_Glossop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/11._With_Eccles_at_the_Kite-Flying_Station_in_Glossop.jpg/440px-11._With_Eccles_at_the_Kite-Flying_Station_in_Glossop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="564" data-file-height="445" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:11._With_Eccles_at_the_Kite-Flying_Station_in_Glossop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ludwig with Eccles at the Kite-Flying Station in <a href="/wiki/Glossop" title="Glossop">Glossop</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Work on the jet-powered propeller proved frustrating for Wittgenstein, who had very little experience working with machinery.<sup id="cite_ref-Mays,_p.137_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mays,_p.137-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Jim Bamber, a British engineer who was his friend and classmate at the time, reported that \xe2\x80\x9cwhen things went wrong, which often occurred, he would throw his arms around, stomp about, and swear volubly in German.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> According to William Eccles, another friend from that period, Wittgenstein then turned to more theoretical work, focusing on the design of the propeller \xe2\x80\x94 a problem that required relatively sophisticated mathematics.<sup id="cite_ref-Mays,_p.137_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mays,_p.137-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was at this time that he became interested in the <a href="/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations of mathematics</a>, particularly after reading <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Principles_of_Mathematics" title="The Principles of Mathematics">The Principles of Mathematics</a></i> (1903), and <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Foundations_of_Arithmetic" title="The Foundations of Arithmetic">The Foundations of Arithmetic</a></i>, vol. 1 (1893) and vol. 2 (1903).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein\'s sister Hermine said he became obsessed with mathematics as a result, and was anyway losing interest in aeronautics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199030_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199030-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> He decided instead that he needed to study logic and the foundations of mathematics, describing himself as in a "constant, indescribable, almost pathological state of agitation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199030_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199030-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In the summer of 1911 he visited Frege at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Jena" title="University of Jena">University of Jena</a> to show him some philosophy of mathematics and logic he had written, and to ask whether it was worth pursuing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199036_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199036-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> He wrote: "I was shown into Frege\'s study. Frege was a small, neat man with a pointed beard who bounced around the room as he talked. He absolutely wiped the floor with me, and I felt very depressed; but at the end he said \'You must come again\', so I cheered up. I had several discussions with him after that. Frege would never talk about anything but logic and mathematics, if I started on some other subject, he would say something polite and then plunge back into logic and mathematics."<sup id="cite_ref-Kanterian36_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kanterian36-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrival_at_Cambridge">Arrival at Cambridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Arrival at Cambridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_1910.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_1910.jpg/220px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein_1910.jpg" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/75/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_1910.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="236" data-file-height="301" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_1910.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein, 1910s</div></div></div>\n<p>Wittgenstein wanted to study with Frege, but Frege suggested he attend the University of Cambridge to study under Russell, so on 18 October 1911 Wittgenstein arrived unannounced at Russell\'s rooms in <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ConnorRobertson_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ConnorRobertson-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Russell was having tea with <a href="/wiki/C._K._Ogden" class="mw-redirect" title="C. K. Ogden">C. K. Ogden</a>, when, according to Russell, "an unknown German appeared, speaking very little English but refusing to speak German. He turned out to be a man who had learned engineering at Charlottenburg, but during this course had acquired, by himself, a passion for the philosophy of mathematics &amp; has now come to Cambridge on purpose to hear me."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199036_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199036-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> He was soon not only attending Russell\'s lectures, but dominating them. The lectures were poorly attended and Russell often found himself lecturing only to <a href="/wiki/C._D._Broad" title="C. D. Broad">C. D. Broad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Harold_Neville" title="Eric Harold Neville">E. H. Neville</a>, and H. T. J. Norton.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199036_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199036-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein started following him after lectures back to his rooms to discuss more philosophy, until it was time for the evening meal in <a href="/wiki/Formal_(university)#Terminology" title="Formal (university)">Hall</a>. Russell grew irritated; he wrote to his lover <a href="/wiki/Lady_Ottoline_Morrell" title="Lady Ottoline Morrell">Lady Ottoline Morrell</a>: "My German friend threatens to be an infliction."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198888-89_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198888-89-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\nRussell soon came to believe that Wittgenstein was a genius, especially after he had examined Wittgenstein\'s written work. He wrote in November 1911 that he had at first thought Wittgenstein might be a crank, but soon decided he was a genius: "Some of his early views made the decision difficult. He maintained, for example, at one time that all existential propositions are meaningless. This was in a lecture room, and I invited him to consider the proposition: \'There is no hippopotamus in this room at present.\' When he refused to believe this, I looked under all the desks without finding one; but he remained unconvinced."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198888_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness198888-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Three months after Wittgenstein\'s arrival Russell told Morrell: "I love him &amp; feel he will solve the problems I am too old to solve ... He is <i>the</i> young man one hopes for."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199041_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199041-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein later told David Pinsent that Russell\xe2\x80\x99s encouragement had proven his salvation, and had ended nine years of loneliness and suffering, during which he had continually thought of suicide. In encouraging him to pursue philosophy and in justifying his inclination to abandon engineering, Russell had, quite literally, saved Wittgenstein\xe2\x80\x99s life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199041_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199041-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The role-reversal between Bertrand Russell and Wittgenstein was soon such that Russell wrote in 1916, after Wittgenstein had criticized Russell\'s own work: "His [Wittenstein]\'s criticism, tho\' I don\'t think you realized it at the time, was an event of first-rate importance in my life, and affected everything I have done since. I saw that he was right, and I saw that I could not hope ever again to do fundamental work in philosophy."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cambridge_Moral_Sciences_Club_and_Apostles">Cambridge Moral Sciences Club and Apostles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cambridge Moral Sciences Club and Apostles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:180px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Russell1907-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Russell1907-2.jpg/178px-Russell1907-2.jpg" width="178" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Russell1907-2.jpg/268px-Russell1907-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/Russell1907-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="316" data-file-height="414" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Russell1907-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>, 1907</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1912 Wittgenstein joined the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Moral_Sciences_Club" title="Cambridge University Moral Sciences Club">Cambridge Moral Sciences Club</a>, an influential discussion group for philosophy dons and students, delivering his first paper there on 29 November that year, a four-minute talk defining philosophy as "all those primitive propositions which are assumed as true without proof by the various sciences."<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> He dominated the society and for a time would stop attending in the early 1930s after complaints that he gave no one else a chance to speak.<sup id="cite_ref-Edmonds22_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edmonds22-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The club became infamous within popular philosophy because of a meeting on 25 October 1946 at <a href="/wiki/R._B._Braithwaite" title="R. B. Braithwaite">Richard Braithwaite</a>\'s rooms in <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College,_Cambridge" title="King&#39;s College, Cambridge">King\'s</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a>, another Viennese philosopher, had been invited as the guest speaker. Popper\'s paper was <i>Are there philosophical problems?</i>, in which he struck up a position against Wittgenstein\'s, contending that problems in philosophy are real, not just linguistic puzzles as Wittgenstein argued. Accounts vary as to what happened next, but Wittgenstein apparently started waving a hot poker, demanding that Popper give him an example of a moral rule. Popper offered one\xe2\x80\x94"Not to threaten visiting speakers with pokers"\xe2\x80\x94at which point Russell told Wittgenstein he had misunderstood and Wittgenstein left. Popper maintained that Wittgenstein \'stormed out\', but it had become accepted practice for him to leave early (because of his aforementioned ability to dominate discussion). It was the only time the philosophers, three of the most eminent in the world, were ever in the same room together.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The minutes record that the meeting was "charged to an unusual degree with a spirit of controversy."<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> also invited him to join the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Apostles" title="Cambridge Apostles">Cambridge Apostles</a>, an elite secret society formed in 1820, which both Russell and <a href="/wiki/G._E._Moore" title="G. E. Moore">G. E. Moore</a> had joined as students, but Wittgenstein did not enjoy it and attended infrequently. Russell had been worried that Wittgenstein would not appreciate the group\'s unseriousness, style of humour, or the fact that the members were in love with one another.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988118_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988118-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He was admitted in 1912 but resigned almost immediately because he could not tolerate the level of discussion; they took him back though in the 1920s when he returned to Cambridge. (He also had trouble tolerating the discussions in the Moral Sciences Club.)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Frustrations_at_Cambridge.2FPre-war"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Frustrations_at_Cambridge/Pre-war">Frustrations at Cambridge/Pre-war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Frustrations at Cambridge/Pre-war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Wittgenstein was quite vocal about his depression in his years at Cambridge, and before he went to war; on many an occasion, he told Russell of his woes. His mental anguish seemed to stem from two sources: his work, and his personal life. Wittgenstein made numerous remarks to Russell about logic driving him mad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199076_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199076-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein also stated to Russell that he "felt the curse of those who have half a talent".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200839_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200839-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> He later expresses this same worry, and tells of being in mediocre spirits due to his lack of progress in his logical work.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Monk writes that Wittgenstein lived and breathed logic, and a temporary lack of inspiration plunged him into despair.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199075_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199075-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Unless he was lying, Wittgenstein tells of his work in logic affecting his mental status in a very extreme way. However, he also tells Russell another story. Around Christmas, in 1913, he writes: "how can I be a logician before I\'m a human being? For the most important thing is coming to terms with myself!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200863_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200863-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> He also tells Russell on an occasion in Russell\xe2\x80\x99s rooms that he was worried about logic and his sins; also, once upon arrival to Russell\'s rooms one night Wittgenstein announced to Russell that he would kill himself once he left.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Of things Wittgenstein personally told Russell, Ludwig\xe2\x80\x99s temperament was also recorded in the diary of David Pinsent. Pinsent writes "I have to be frightfully careful and tolerant when he gets these sulky fits", and "I am afraid he is in an even more sensitive neurotic state just now than usual", when talking about Wittgenstein\'s emotional fluctuations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199085_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199085-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_orientation_and_relationship_with_David_Pinsent">Sexual orientation and relationship with David Pinsent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Sexual orientation and relationship with David Pinsent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:15._David_Pinsent.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/15._David_Pinsent.jpg/180px-15._David_Pinsent.jpg" width="180" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/15._David_Pinsent.jpg/270px-15._David_Pinsent.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/15._David_Pinsent.jpg/360px-15._David_Pinsent.jpg 2x" data-file-width="369" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:15._David_Pinsent.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Pinsent" title="David Pinsent">David Pinsent</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_with_friends_drinking_coffee.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Wittgenstein_with_friends_drinking_coffee.jpg/240px-Wittgenstein_with_friends_drinking_coffee.jpg" width="240" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Wittgenstein_with_friends_drinking_coffee.jpg/360px-Wittgenstein_with_friends_drinking_coffee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Wittgenstein_with_friends_drinking_coffee.jpg/480px-Wittgenstein_with_friends_drinking_coffee.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1428" data-file-height="854" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_with_friends_drinking_coffee.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein sitting with his friends and family in Vienna. Marguerite Respinger sits at the end of the left and the sculpture he made of her sits behind him on the mantel-place</div></div></div>\n<p>Wittgenstein had romantic relations with both men and women. He is generally believed to have fallen in love with at least three men, and had a relationship with the latter two: <a href="/wiki/David_Pinsent" title="David Pinsent">David Hume Pinsent</a> in 1912, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Skinner" title="Francis Skinner">Francis Skinner</a> in 1930, and Ben Richards in the late 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990583-586_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990583-586-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> He later revealed that, as a teenager in Vienna, he had had an affair with a woman.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990369_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990369-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, in the 1920s Wittgenstein fell in love with a young Swiss woman, Marguerite Respinger, modelling a sculpture of her and proposing marriage, albeit on condition that they would not have children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990238-240,_318_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990238-240,_318-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wittgenstein\'s relationship with David Pinsent (1891\xe2\x80\x931918) occurred during an intellectually formative period, and is well documented. Bertrand Russell introduced Wittgenstein to Pinsent in the summer of 1912. A mathematics undergraduate and descendant of <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, Pinsent soon became Wittgenstein\'s closest friend.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The men worked together on experiments in the psychology laboratory about the role of rhythm in the appreciation of music, and Wittgenstein delivered a paper on the subject to the <a href="/wiki/British_Psychological_Society" title="British Psychological Society">British Psychological Association</a> in Cambridge in 1912. They also travelled together, including to Iceland in September 1912\xe2\x80\x94the expenses paid by Wittgenstein, including <a href="/wiki/First_class_travel" title="First class travel">first class travel</a>, the hiring of a private train, and new clothes and spending money for Pinsent. In addition to Iceland, Wittgenstein and Pinsent traveled to Norway in 1913. Upon determining their destination, Wittgenstein and Pinsent visited a tourist office in search for a location that would fulfill the following criteria \xe2\x80\x93 small village located on a Fjord, a location away from tourists, and a peaceful destination to allow them to study logic and law.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Suggesting <a href="/wiki/%C3%98ystese" title="\xc3\x98ystese">\xc3\x98ystese</a>, Wittgenstein and Pinsent arrived in the small village on 4 September 1913. With their vacation lasting almost three weeks, Wittgenstein was able to work vigorously on his studies.
