The immense progress on logic during their stay led Wittgenstein to express to Pinsent his idea to leave Cambridge and return to Norway to continue his work on logic.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Pinsent\'s diaries provide valuable insights into Wittgenstein\'s personality \xe2\x80\x93 sensitive, nervous and attuned to the tiniest slight or change in mood from Pinsent.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>  Pinsent also writes of Wittgenstein being "absolutely sulky and snappish" at times, as well.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199085_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199085-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In his diaries Pinsent wrote about shopping for furniture with Wittgenstein in Cambridge when the latter was given rooms in Trinity; most of what they found in the stores was not minimalist enough for Wittgenstein\'s aesthetics: "I went and helped him interview a lot of furniture at various shops ... It was rather amusing: he is terribly fastidious and we led the shopman a frightful dance, Vittgenstein [sic] ejaculating "No\xe2\x80\x94Beastly!" to 90 percent of what he shewed [archaic spelling] us!"<sup id="cite_ref-Kanterian40_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kanterian40-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He wrote in May 1912 that Wittgenstein had just begun to study the history of philosophy: "He expresses the most naive surprise that all the philosophers he once worshipped in ignorance are after all stupid and dishonest and make disgusting mistakes!"<sup id="cite_ref-Kanterian40_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kanterian40-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The last time they saw each other was on 8 October 1913 at Lordswood House in Birmingham, then residence of the Pinsent family: "I got up at 6.15 to see Ludwig off. He had to go very early \xe2\x80\x93 back to Cambridge \xe2\x80\x93 as he has lots to do there. I saw him off from the house in a taxi at 7.0 \xe2\x80\x93 to catch a 7.30 am train from New St Station. It was sad parting from him."<sup id="cite_ref-Wright_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein left to live in Norway.\n</p>\n<h2><span id="1913.E2.80.931920:_World_War_I_and_the_Tractatus"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1913\xe2\x80\x931920:_World_War_I_and_the_Tractatus">1913\xe2\x80\x931920: World War I and the <i>Tractatus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 1913\xe2\x80\x931920: World War I and the Tractatus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Work_on_Logik">Work on <i>Logik</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Work on Logik">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg/220px-Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg/330px-Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg/440px-Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_notes_1914.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Entries from October 1914 in Wittgenstein\'s diary, on display at the <a href="/wiki/Wren_Library" title="Wren Library">Wren Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Karl Wittgenstein died on 20 January 1913, and after receiving his inheritance Wittgenstein became one of the wealthiest men in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199071_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199071-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> He donated some of his money, at first anonymously, to Austrian artists and writers, including <a href="/wiki/Rainer_Maria_Rilke" title="Rainer Maria Rilke">Rainer Maria Rilke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georg_Trakl" title="Georg Trakl">Georg Trakl</a>. Trakl requested to meet his benefactor but in 1914 when Wittgenstein went to visit, Trakl had already killed himself.  Wittgenstein came to feel that he could not get to the heart of his most fundamental questions while surrounded by other academics, and so in 1913 he retreated to the village of <a href="/wiki/Skjolden" title="Skjolden">Skjolden</a> in Norway, where he rented the second floor of a house for the winter. He later saw this as one of the most productive periods of his life, writing <i>Logik</i> (<i>Notes on Logic</i>), the predecessor of much of the <i>Tractatus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ConnorRobertson_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ConnorRobertson-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> While in Norway, Wittgenstein learned <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a> to converse with the local villagers, and <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> to read the works of the Danish philosopher <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="S\xc3\xb8ren Kierkegaard">S\xc3\xb8ren Kierkegaard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was during this time that Wittgenstein began addressing what he considered to be a central issue in <i>Notes on Logic</i>, a general decision procedure for determining the truth value of logical propositions which would stem from a single primitive proposition. He became convinced during this time that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]ll the propositions of logic are generalizations of tautologies and all generalizations of tautologies are generalizations of logic. There are no other logical propositions.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200858_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200858-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Based on this, Wittgenstein argued that propositions of logic express their truth or falsehood in the sign itself, and one need not know anything about the constituent parts of the proposition to determine it true or false. Rather, one simply need identify the statement as a tautology (true), a contradiction (false), or neither.\n</p><p>The problem lay in forming a primitive proposition which encompassed this and would act as the basis for all of logic. As he stated in correspondence with Russell in late 1913, \xe2\x80\x9cThe big question now is, how must a system of signs be constituted in order to make every tautology recognizable as such IN ONE AND THE SAME WAY? This is the fundamental problem of logic!\xe2\x80\x9d <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200859_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness200859-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The importance Wittgenstein placed upon this fundamental problem was so great that he believed if he did not solve it, he had no reason or right to live.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199096_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199096-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Despite this apparent life-or-death importance, Wittgenstein had given up on this primitive proposition by the time of the writing of the <i>Tractatus</i>. The <i>Tractatus</i> does not offer any general process for identifying propositions as tautologies; in a simpler manner, \xe2\x80\x9cEvery tautology itself shows that it is a tautology.\xe2\x80\x9d<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> This shift to understanding tautologies through mere identification or recognition occurred in 1914 when Moore was called on by Wittgenstein to assist him in dictating his notes.\n</p><p>At Wittgenstein\'s insistence, Moore, who was now a Cambridge don, visited him in Norway in 1914, reluctantly because Wittgenstein exhausted him. <a href="/wiki/David_Edmonds_(philosopher)" title="David Edmonds (philosopher)">David Edmonds</a> and John Eidinow write that Wittgenstein regarded Moore, an internationally known philosopher, as an example of how far someone could get in life with "absolutely no intelligence whatever."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990262_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990262-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In Norway it was clear that Moore was expected to act as Wittgenstein\'s secretary, taking down his notes, with Wittgenstein falling into a rage when Moore got something wrong.<sup id="cite_ref-Edmonds45_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edmonds45-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> When he returned to Cambridge, Moore asked the university to consider accepting <i>Logik</i> as sufficient for a bachelor\'s degree, but they refused, saying it wasn\'t formatted properly: no footnotes, no preface. Wittgenstein was furious, writing to Moore in May 1914: "If I am not worth your making an exception for me <i>even in some STUPID details</i> then I may as well go to Hell directly; and if I <i>am</i> worth it and you don\'t do it then\xe2\x80\x94by God\xe2\x80\x94<i>you</i> might go there."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990103_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990103-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Moore was apparently distraught; he wrote in his diary that he felt sick and could not get the letter out of his head.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988200_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGuinness1988200-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The two did not speak again until 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-Edmonds45_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edmonds45-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_service">Military service</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Military service">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25,_Isonzo-Schlacht,_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg/150px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg" width="150" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg/225px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg/300px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25%2C_Isonzo-Schlacht%2C_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg 2x" data-file-width="589" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1970-073-25,_Isonzo-Schlacht,_Trainkolonne_am_Moistroka-Pass.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Austro-Hungarian supply line over the <a href="/wiki/Vr%C5%A1i%C4%8D_Pass" title="Vr\xc5\xa1i\xc4\x8d Pass">Vr\xc5\xa1i\xc4\x8d Pass</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Campaign_(World_War_I)" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian Campaign (World War I)">Italian front</a>, October 1917</div></div></div>\n<p>On the outbreak of World War I, Wittgenstein immediately volunteered for the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Army" title="Austro-Hungarian Army">Austro-Hungarian Army</a>, despite being eligible for a medical exemption.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>  He served first on a ship and then in an artillery workshop \'several miles from the action\'.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> He was wounded in an accidental explosion, and hospitalised to <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Krak\xc3\xb3w">Krak\xc3\xb3w</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>  In March 1916, he was posted to a fighting unit on the front line of the Russian front, as part of the <a href="/wiki/7th_Army_(Austria-Hungary)" title="7th Army (Austria-Hungary)">Austrian 7th Army</a>, where his unit was involved in some of the heaviest fighting, defending against the <a href="/wiki/Brusilov_Offensive" title="Brusilov Offensive">Brusilov Offensive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990137-142_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990137-142-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein directed the fire of his own artillery from an observation post in no-man\'s land against Allied troops \xe2\x80\x93 one of the most dangerous jobs there was, since he was targeted by enemy fire.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>  In action against British troops, he was decorated with the <a href="/wiki/Military_Merit_Medal_(Austria%E2%80%93Hungary)" class="mw-redirect" title="Military Merit Medal (Austria\xe2\x80\x93Hungary)">Military Merit</a> with Swords on the Ribbon, and was commended by the army for "His exceptionally courageous behaviour, calmness, sang-froid, and heroism," that "won the total admiration of the troops."<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> In January 1917, he was sent as a member of a <a href="/wiki/Howitzer" title="Howitzer">howitzer</a> regiment to the Russian front, where he won several more medals for bravery including the <a href="/wiki/Medal_for_Bravery_(Austria-Hungary)" title="Medal for Bravery (Austria-Hungary)">Silver Medal for Valour</a>, First Class.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990137_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990137-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In 1918, he was promoted to lieutenant and sent to the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Campaign_(World_War_I)" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian Campaign (World War I)">Italian front</a> as part of an artillery regiment. For his part in the final Austrian offensive of June 1918, he was recommended for the Gold Medal for Valour, one of the highest honours in the Austrian army, but was instead awarded the Band of the Military Service Medal with Swords&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 it being decided that this particular action, although extraordinarily brave, had been insufficiently consequential to merit the highest honour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990154_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990154-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:149px;"><a href="/wiki/File:27._Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_military_identity_card_during_the_First_World_War.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/27._Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_military_identity_card_during_the_First_World_War.jpg/147px-27._Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_military_identity_card_during_the_First_World_War.jpg" width="147" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/21/27._Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_military_identity_card_during_the_First_World_War.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="195" data-file-height="303" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:27._Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_military_identity_card_during_the_First_World_War.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein\xe2\x80\x99s military identity card during the First World War</div></div></div>\n<p>Throughout the war, he kept notebooks in which he frequently wrote philosophical reflections alongside personal remarks, including his contempt for the character of the other soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-Klagge2010_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klagge2010-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> He discovered <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a>\'s 1896 <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gospel_in_Brief" title="The Gospel in Brief">The Gospel in Brief</a></i> at a bookshop in <a href="/wiki/Tarn%C3%B3w" title="Tarn\xc3\xb3w">Tarn\xc3\xb3w</a>, and carried it everywhere, recommending it to anyone in distress, to the point where he became known to his fellow soldiers as "the man with the gospels."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk199044,_116,_382\xe2\x80\x93384_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk199044,_116,_382\xe2\x80\x93384-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The extent to which <i>The Gospel in Brief</i> influenced Wittgenstein can be seen in the <i>Tractatus</i>, in the unique way both books number their sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> 1916 Wittgenstein read <a href="/wiki/Dostoevsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Dostoevsky">Dostoevsky</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brothers_Karamazov" title="The Brothers Karamazov">The Brothers Karamazov</a></i> so often that he knew whole passages of it by heart, particularly the speeches of the elder Zosima, who represented for him a powerful Christian ideal, a holy man "who could see directly into the souls of other people."<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iain_King" title="Iain King">Iain King</a> has suggested his writing changed substantially in 1916, when he started confronting much greater dangers during frontline fighting.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>  Russell said he returned from the war a changed man, one with a deeply mystical and ascetic attitude.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990183_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990183-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Completion_of_the_Tractatus">Completion of the <i>Tractatus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Completion of the Tractatus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_family_Vienna_1917.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Wittgenstein_family_Vienna_1917.jpg/220px-Wittgenstein_family_Vienna_1917.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Wittgenstein_family_Vienna_1917.jpg/330px-Wittgenstein_family_Vienna_1917.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Wittgenstein_family_Vienna_1917.jpg/440px-Wittgenstein_family_Vienna_1917.jpg 2x" data-file-width="630" data-file-height="454" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_family_Vienna_1917.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Wittgenstein family in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, Summer 1917, with Kurt (furthest left) and Wittgenstein (furthest right) in officers\' uniforms.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the summer of 1918 Wittgenstein took military leave and went to stay in one of his family\'s Vienna summer houses, Neuwaldegg. It was there in August 1918 that he completed the <i>Tractatus</i>, which he submitted with the title <i>Der Satz</i> (German: proposition, sentence, phrase, set, but also "leap") to the publishers Jahoda and Siegel.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartley33_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartley33-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A series of events around this time left him deeply upset. On 13 August, his uncle Paul died. On 25 October, he learned that Jahoda and Siegel had decided not to publish the <i>Tractatus</i>, and on 27 October, his brother Kurt killed himself, the third of his brothers to commit suicide. It was around this time he received a letter from David Pinsent\'s mother to say that Pinsent had been killed in a plane crash on 8 May.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein was distraught to the point of being suicidal. He was sent back to the Italian front after his leave and, as a result of the defeat of the Austrian army, he was captured by <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied forces</a> on 3 November in <a href="/wiki/Trentino" title="Trentino">Trentino</a>. He subsequently spent nine months in an Italian prisoner of war camp.\n</p><p>He returned to his family in Vienna on 25 August 1919, by all accounts physically and mentally spent. He apparently talked incessantly about suicide, terrifying his sisters and brother Paul. He decided to do two things: to enroll in teacher training college as an elementary school teacher, and to get rid of his fortune. In 1914, it had been providing him with an income of 300,000 <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_krone" title="Austro-Hungarian krone">Kronen</a> a year, but by 1919 was worth a great deal more, with a sizable portfolio of investments in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. He divided it among his siblings, except for Margarete, insisting that it not be held in trust for him. His family saw him as ill, and acquiesced.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartley33_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartley33-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="1920.E2.80.931928:_Teaching.2C_the_Tractatus.2C_Haus_Wittgenstein"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1920\xe2\x80\x931928:_Teaching,_the_Tractatus,_Haus_Wittgenstein">1920\xe2\x80\x931928: Teaching, the <i>Tractatus</i>, Haus Wittgenstein</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 1920\xe2\x80\x931928: Teaching, the Tractatus, Haus Wittgenstein">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teacher_training_in_Vienna">Teacher training in Vienna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Teacher training in Vienna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In September 1919 he enrolled in the <i>Lehrerbildungsanstalt</i> (teacher training college) in the <i>Kundmanngasse</i> in Vienna. His sister Hermine said that Wittgenstein working as an elementary teacher was like using a precision instrument to open crates, but the family decided not to interfere.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990169_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990169-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bernhard" title="Thomas Bernhard">Thomas Bernhard</a>, more critically, wrote of this period in Wittgenstein\'s life: "the multi-millionaire as a village schoolmaster is surely a piece of perversity."<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teaching_posts_in_Austria">Teaching posts in Austria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Teaching posts in Austria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p>\nIn the summer of 1920, Wittgenstein worked as a gardener for a monastery. At first he applied, under a false name, for a teaching post at Reichenau, was awarded the job, but he declined it when his identity was discovered. As a teacher, he wished to no longer be recognized as a member of the famous Wittgenstein family. In response, his brother Paul wrote: </p><blockquote><p>"It is out of the question, really completely out of the question, that anybody bearing our name and whose elegant and gentle upbringing can be seen a thousand paces off, would not be identified as a member of our family... That one can neither simulate nor dissimulate anything including a refined education I need hardly tell you."<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>In 1920, Wittgenstein was given his first job as a primary school teacher in <a href="/wiki/Trattenbach" title="Trattenbach">Trattenbach</a>, under his real name, in a remote village of a few hundred people. His first letters describe it as beautiful, but in October 1921, he wrote to Russell: "I am still at Trattenbach, surrounded, as ever, by odiousness and baseness. I know that human beings on the average are not worth much anywhere, but here they are much more good-for-nothing and irresponsible than elsewhere."<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> He was soon the object of gossip among the villagers, who found him eccentric at best. He did not get on well with the other teachers; when he found his lodgings too noisy, he made a bed for himself in the school kitchen. He was an enthusiastic teacher, offering late-night extra tuition to several of the students, something that did not endear him to the parents, though some of them came to adore him; his sister Hermine occasionally watched him teach and said the students "literally crawled over each other in their desire to be chosen for answers or demonstrations."<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>To the less able, it seems that he became something of a tyrant. The first two hours of each day were devoted to mathematics, hours that Monk writes some of the pupils recalled years later with horror.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990195_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990195-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> They reported that he caned the boys and boxed their ears, and also that he pulled the girls\' hair;<sup id="cite_ref-Bartley107_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartley107-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> This was not unusual at the time for boys, but for the villagers he went too far in doing it to the girls too; girls were not expected to understand algebra, much less have their ears boxed over it. The violence apart, Monk writes that he quickly became a village legend, shouting "<i>Krautsalat!</i>" ("coleslaw" \xe2\x80\x93 i.e. shredded cabbage) when the headmaster played the piano, and "Nonsense!" when a priest was answering children\'s questions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990196,_198_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990196,_198-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Publication_of_the_Tractatus">Publication of the <i>Tractatus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Publication of the Tractatus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Wittgenstein2.jpg/300px-Wittgenstein2.jpg" width="300" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Wittgenstein2.jpg/450px-Wittgenstein2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Wittgenstein2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="459" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ludwig Wittgenstein, schoolteacher, c. 1922</div></div></div><p>While Wittgenstein was living in isolation in rural Austria, the <i><a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Logico-Philosophicus" title="Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus">Tractatus</a></i> was published to considerable interest, first in German in 1921 as <i>Logisch-Philosophische Abhandlung</i>, part of <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Ostwald" title="Wilhelm Ostwald">Wilhelm Ostwald</a>\'s journal <i>Annalen der Naturphilosophie</i>, though Wittgenstein was not happy with the result and called it a pirate edition. Russell had agreed to write an introduction to explain why it was important, because it was otherwise unlikely to have been published: it was difficult if not impossible to understand, and Wittgenstein was unknown in philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> In a letter to Russell, Wittgenstein wrote "The main point is the theory of what can be expressed (gesagt) by prop[osition]s\xe2\x80\x94i.e. by language\xe2\x80\x94(and, which comes to the same thing, what can be <i>thought</i>) and what can not be expressed by pro[position]s, but only shown (gezeigt); which, I believe, is the cardinal problem of philosophy."<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> But Wittgenstein was not happy with Russell\'s help. He had lost faith in Russell, finding him glib and his philosophy mechanistic, and felt he had fundamentally misunderstood the <i>Tractatus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><table class="rquote pullquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">\n\n<tbody><tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">\n<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="padding:0 10px;"><i>The whole modern conception of the world is founded on the illusion that the so-called laws of nature are the explanations of natural phenomena.</i>\n<p><i>Thus people today stop at the laws of nature, treating them as something inviolable, just as God and Fate were treated in past ages. And in fact both were right and both wrong; though the view of the ancients is clearer insofar as they have an acknowledged terminus, while the modern system tries to make it look as if everything were explained.</i>\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:normal; text-align:right;"><cite>\xe2\x80\x94&#160;Wittgenstein, <a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Logico-Philosophicus" title="Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus">Tractatus</a>, 6.371-2</cite>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>An English translation was prepared in Cambridge by <a href="/wiki/Frank_P._Ramsey" title="Frank P. Ramsey">Frank Ramsey</a>, a mathematics undergraduate at King\'s commissioned by <a href="/wiki/C._K._Ogden" class="mw-redirect" title="C. K. Ogden">C. K. Ogden</a>. It was Moore who suggested <i>Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus</i> for the title, an allusion to <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>\'s <i>Tractatus Theologico-Politicus.</i> Initially there were difficulties in finding a publisher for the English edition too, because Wittgenstein was insisting it appear without Russell\'s introduction; Cambridge University Press turned it down for that reason. Finally in 1922 an agreement was reached with Wittgenstein that Kegan Paul would print a bilingual edition with Russell\'s introduction and the Ramsey-Ogden translation.<sup id="cite_ref-archiveTractatus_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archiveTractatus-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> This is the translation that was approved by Wittgenstein, but it is problematic in a number of ways. Wittgenstein\'s English was poor at the time, and Ramsey was a teenager who had only recently learned German, so philosophers often prefer to use a 1961 translation by <a href="/wiki/David_Pears" title="David Pears">David Pears</a> and Brian McGuinness.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An aim of the <i>Tractatus</i> is to reveal the relationship between language and the world: what can be said about it, and what can only be shown. Wittgenstein argues that the logical structure of language provides the limits of meaning. The limits of language, for Wittgenstein, are the limits of philosophy. Much of philosophy involves attempts to say the unsayable: "What we can say at all can be said clearly," he argues. Anything beyond that\xe2\x80\x94religion, ethics, aesthetics, the mystical\xe2\x80\x94cannot be discussed. They are not in themselves nonsensical, but any statement about them must be.<sup id="cite_ref-Grayling16_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grayling16-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> He wrote in the preface: "The book will, therefore, draw a limit to thinking, or rather\xe2\x80\x94not to thinking, but to the expression of thoughts; for, in order to draw a limit to thinking we should have to be able to think both sides of this limit (we should therefore have to be able to think what cannot be thought)."<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The book is 75 pages long\xe2\x80\x94"As to the shortness of the book, I am <i>awfully sorry for it</i> ... If you were to squeeze me like a lemon you would get nothing more out of me," he told Ogden\xe2\x80\x94and presents seven numbered propositions (1\xe2\x80\x937), with various sub-levels (1, 1.1, 1.11):<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ol><li><i>Die Welt ist alles, was der Fall ist</i>.\n<dl><dd>The world is everything that is the case.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl></li>\n<li><i>Was der Fall ist, die Tatsache, ist das Bestehen von Sachverhalten</i>.\n<dl><dd>What is the case, the fact, is the existence of atomic facts.</dd></dl></li>\n<li><i>Das logische Bild der Tatsachen ist der Gedanke</i>.\n<dl><dd>The logical picture of the facts is the thought.</dd></dl></li>\n<li><i>Der Gedanke ist der sinnvolle Satz</i>.\n<dl><dd>The thought is the significant proposition.</dd></dl></li>\n<li><i>Der Satz ist eine Wahrheitsfunktion der Elementars\xc3\xa4tze</i>.\n<dl><dd>Propositions are truth-functions of elementary propositions.</dd></dl></li>\n<li><i>Die allgemeine Form der Wahrheitsfunktion ist: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle [{\\bar {p}},{\\bar {\\xi }},N({\\bar {\\xi }})]}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mo stretchy="false">[</mo>\n        <mrow \xce\xbe --></mi>\n              <mo stretchy="false">&#x00AF;<!-- \xc2\xaf --></mo>\n            </mover>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo>,</mo>\n        <mi>N</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mover>\n              <mi>&#x03BE;<!-- \xce\xbe --></mi>\n              <mo stretchy="false">&#x00AF;<!-- \xc2\xaf --></mo>\n            </mover>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2280fc5b18f2a18924aeac15d592429f9a30389c" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.45ex; height:3.009ex;" alt="[\\bar p,\\bar\\xi, N(\\bar\\xi)]"/></span>. Dies ist die allgemeine Form des Satzes</i>.\n<dl><dd>The general form of a truth-function is: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\\displaystyle [{\\bar {p}},{\\bar {\\xi }},N({\\bar {\\xi }})]}">\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mo stretchy="false">[</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mover>\n              <mi>p</mi>\n              <mo stretchy="false">&#x00AF;<!-- \xc2\xaf --></mo>\n            </mover>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo>,</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mover>\n              <mi>&#x03BE;<!-- \xce\xbe --></mi>\n              <mo stretchy="false">&#x00AF;<!-- \xc2\xaf --></mo>\n            </mover>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo>,</mo>\n        <mi>N</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mover>\n              <mi>&#x03BE;<!-- \xce\xbe --></mi>\n              <mo stretchy="false">&#x00AF;<!-- \xc2\xaf --></mo>\n            </mover>\n          </mrow>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mo stretchy="false">]</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle [{\\bar {p}},{\\bar {\\xi }},N({\\bar {\\xi }})]}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2280fc5b18f2a18924aeac15d592429f9a30389c" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:11.45ex; height:3.009ex;" alt="[\\bar p,\\bar\\xi, N(\\bar\\xi)]"/></span>. This is the general form of proposition.</dd></dl></li>\n<li><i>Wovon man nicht sprechen kann, dar\xc3\xbcber mu\xc3\x9f man schweigen</i>.\n<dl><dd>Whereof one cannot speak, thereof one must be silent.</dd></dl></li></ol>\n<h3><span id="Visit_from_Frank_Ramsey.2C_Puchberg"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Visit_from_Frank_Ramsey,_Puchberg">Visit from Frank Ramsey, Puchberg</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Visit from Frank Ramsey, Puchberg">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg/170px-Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg/255px-Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg/340px-Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2142" data-file-height="2856" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Puchberg_am_Schneeberg-view.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Frank_P._Ramsey" title="Frank P. Ramsey">Frank P. Ramsey</a> visited Wittgenstein in <a href="/wiki/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg" title="Puchberg am Schneeberg">Puchberg am Schneeberg</a> in September 1923.</div></div></div>\n<p>In September 1922 he moved to a secondary school in a nearby village, <a href="/wiki/Hassbach_(Lower_Austria)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hassbach (Lower Austria)">Hassbach</a>, but considered the people there just as bad\xe2\x80\x94"These people are not human <i>at all</i> but loathsome worms," he wrote to a friend\xe2\x80\x94and he left after a month. In November he began work at another primary school, this time in <a href="/wiki/Puchberg_am_Schneeberg" title="Puchberg am Schneeberg">Puchberg</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Schneeberg_(Alps)" title="Schneeberg (Alps)">Schneeberg</a> mountains. There, he told Russell, the villagers were "one-quarter animal and three-quarters human."\n</p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Frank_P._Ramsey" title="Frank P. Ramsey">Frank P. Ramsey</a> visited him on 17 September 1923 to discuss the <i>Tractatus</i>; he had agreed to write a review of it for <i>Mind</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990212,_214\xe2\x80\x93216,_220\xe2\x80\x93221._167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990212,_214\xe2\x80\x93216,_220\xe2\x80\x93221.-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> He reported in a letter home that Wittgenstein was living frugally in one tiny whitewashed room that only had space for a bed, washstand, a small table, and one small hard chair. Ramsey shared an evening meal with him of coarse bread, butter, and cocoa. Wittgenstein\'s school hours were eight to twelve or one, and he had afternoons free.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> After Ramsey returned to Cambridge a long campaign began among Wittgenstein\'s friends to persuade him to return to Cambridge and away from what they saw as a hostile environment for him. He was accepting no help even from his family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990212_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990212-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Ramsey wrote to <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a>: </p><blockquote><p>"[Wittgenstein\'s family] are very rich and extremely anxious to give him money or do anything for him in any way, and he rejects all their advances; even Christmas presents or presents of invalid\'s food, when he is ill, he sends back. And this is not because they aren\'t on good terms but because he won\'t have any money he hasn\'t earned ... It is an awful pity."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990212_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990212-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h3><span id="Haidbauer_incident.2C_Otterthal"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Haidbauer_incident,_Otterthal">Haidbauer incident, Otterthal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Haidbauer incident, Otterthal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haidbauer_incident" title="Haidbauer incident">Haidbauer incident</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:31._Wittgenstein,_1925.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/31._Wittgenstein%2C_1925.jpg/220px-31._Wittgenstein%2C_1925.jpg" width="220" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/31._Wittgenstein%2C_1925.jpg/330px-31._Wittgenstein%2C_1925.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8c/31._Wittgenstein%2C_1925.jpg 2x" data-file-width="388" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:31._Wittgenstein,_1925.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein, 1925</div></div></div>\n<p>He moved schools again in September 1924, this time to <a href="/wiki/Otterthal" title="Otterthal">Otterthal</a>, near Trattenbach; the socialist headmaster, Josef Putre, was someone Wittgenstein had become friends with while at Trattenbach. While he was there, he wrote a 42-page pronunciation and spelling dictionary for the children, <i>W\xc3\xb6rterbuch f\xc3\xbcr Volksschulen</i>, published in Vienna in 1926 by H\xc3\xb6lder-Pichler-Tempsky, the only book of his apart from the <i>Tractatus</i> that was published in his lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-archiveTractatus_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archiveTractatus-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> A first edition sold in 2005 for \xc2\xa375,000.<sup id="cite_ref-Ezard_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ezard-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An incident occurred in April 1926 and became known as <i>Der Vorfall Haidbauer</i> (the Haidbauer incident). Josef Haidbauer was an 11-year-old pupil whose father had died and whose mother worked as a local maid. He was a slow learner, and one day Wittgenstein hit him two or three times on the head, causing him to collapse. Wittgenstein carried him to the headmaster\'s office, then quickly left the school, bumping into a parent, Herr Piribauer, on the way out. Piribauer had been sent for by the children when they saw Haidbauer collapse; Wittgenstein had previously pulled Piribauer\'s daughter, Hermine, so hard by the ears that her ears had bled.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990224,_232\xe2\x80\x93233_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990224,_232\xe2\x80\x93233-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> Piribauer said that when he met Wittgenstein in the hall that day: "I called him all the names under the sun. I told him he wasn\'t a teacher, he was an animal-trainer! And that I was going to fetch the police right away!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990225_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990225-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Piribauer tried to have Wittgenstein arrested, but the village\'s police station was empty, and when he tried again the next day he was told Wittgenstein had disappeared. On 28 April 1926, Wittgenstein handed in his resignation to Wilhelm Kundt, a local school inspector, who tried to persuade him to stay; however, Wittgenstein was adamant that his days as a schoolteacher were over.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990225_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990225-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Proceedings were initiated in May, and the judge ordered a psychiatric report; in August 1926 a letter to Wittgenstein from a friend, Ludwig H\xc3\xa4nsel, indicates that hearings were ongoing, but nothing is known about the case after that. Alexander Waugh writes that Wittgenstein\'s family and their money may have had a hand in covering things up. Waugh writes that Haidbauer died shortly afterwards of haemophilia; Monk says he died when he was 14 of <a href="/wiki/Leukaemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Leukaemia">leukaemia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ten years later, in 1936, as part of a series of "confessions" he engaged in that year, Wittgenstein appeared without warning at the village saying he wanted to confess personally and ask for pardon from the children he had hit. He visited at least four of the children, including Hermine Piribauer, who apparently replied only with a "Ja, ja," though other former students were more hospitable. Monk writes that the purpose of these confessions was not "to hurt his pride, as a form of punishment; it was to dismantle it\xe2\x80\x94to remove a barrier, as it were, that stood in the way of honest and decent thought." Of the apologies, Wittgenstein wrote, "This brought me into more settled waters... and to greater seriousness."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990370-371_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990370-371-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Vienna_Circle">The Vienna Circle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The Vienna Circle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Circle" title="Vienna Circle">Vienna Circle</a></div>\n<p>The <i>Tractatus</i> was now the subject of much debate amongst philosophers, and Wittgenstein was a figure of increasing international fame. In particular, a discussion group of philosophers, scientists and mathematicians, known as the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Circle" title="Vienna Circle">Vienna Circle</a>, had built up largely as a result of the inspiration they had been given by reading the <i>Tractatus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990212_169-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990212-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> While it is commonly assumed that Wittgenstein was a part of the Vienna Circle, in reality, this was not actually the case. German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Oswald_Hanfling" title="Oswald Hanfling">Oswald Hanfling</a> writes bluntly: "Wittgenstein was never a member of the Circle, though he was in Vienna during much of the time. Yet his influence on the Circle\'s thought was at least as important as that of any of its members."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> From 1926, with the members of the Vienna Circle, Wittgenstein would take part in many discussions. However, during these discussions, it soon became evident that Wittgenstein held a different attitude towards philosophy than the members of the Circle whom his work had inspired. For example, during meetings of the Vienna Circle, he would express his disagreement with the group\'s misreading of his work by turning his back to them and reading poetry aloud.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>  In his autobiography, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Carnap" title="Rudolf Carnap">Rudolf Carnap</a> describes Wittgenstein as the thinker who gave him the greatest inspiration. However, he also wrote that "there was a striking difference between Wittgenstein\'s attitude toward philosophical problems and that of Schlick and myself. Our attitude toward philosophical problems was not very different from that which scientists have toward their problems." As for Wittgenstein:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>His point of view and his attitude toward people and problems, even theoretical problems, were much more similar to those of a creative artist than to those of a scientist; one might almost say, similar to those of a religious prophet or a seer... When finally, sometimes after a prolonged arduous effort, his answers came forth, his statement stood before us like a newly created piece of art or a divine revelation ... the impression he made on us was as if insight came to him as through divine inspiration, so that we could not help feeling that any sober rational comment or analysis of it would be a profanation.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Haus_Wittgenstein">Haus Wittgenstein</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Haus Wittgenstein">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haus_Wittgenstein" title="Haus Wittgenstein">Haus Wittgenstein</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Haus_Wittgenstein,_Stonborough_House,_Vienna.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg/150px-Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg/225px-Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg/300px-Haus_Wittgenstein%2C_Stonborough_House%2C_Vienna.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="720" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Haus_Wittgenstein,_Stonborough_House,_Vienna.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein worked on <a href="/wiki/Haus_Wittgenstein" title="Haus Wittgenstein">Haus Wittgenstein</a> between 1926 and 1929.</div></div></div>\n<table class="rquote pullquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">\n\n<tbody><tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">\n<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="padding:0 10px;">"I am not interested in erecting a building, but in [...] presenting to myself the foundations of all possible buildings."\n</td>\n<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:normal; text-align:right;"><cite>\xe2\x80\x94&#160;Wittgenstein<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In 1926 Wittgenstein was again working as a gardener for a number of months, this time at the monastery of H\xc3\xbctteldorf, where he had also inquired about becoming a monk. His sister, Margaret, invited him to help with the design of her new townhouse in Vienna\'s <i>Kundmanngasse</i>. Wittgenstein, his friend <a href="/wiki/Paul_Engelmann" title="Paul Engelmann">Paul Engelmann</a>, and a team of architects developed a spare modernist house. In particular, Wittgenstein focused on the windows, doors, and radiators, demanding that every detail be exactly as he specified. When the house was nearly finished Wittgenstein had an entire ceiling raised 30mm so that the room had the exact proportions he wanted. Monk writes that "This is not so marginal as it may at first appear, for it is precisely these details that lend what is otherwise a rather plain, even ugly house its distinctive beauty."<sup id="cite_ref-Jeffries_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffries-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It took him a year to design the door handles and another to design the radiators. Each window was covered by a metal screen that weighed 150&#160;kg, moved by a pulley Wittgenstein designed. Bernhard Leitner, author of <i>The Architecture of Ludwig Wittgenstein</i>, said there is barely anything comparable in the history of interior design: "It is as ingenious as it is expensive. A metal curtain that could be lowered into the floor."<sup id="cite_ref-Jeffries_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffries-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The house was finished by December 1928 and the family gathered there at Christmas to celebrate its completion. Wittgenstein\'s sister Hermine wrote: "Even though I admired the house very much....It seemed indeed to be much more a dwelling for the gods."<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein said "the house I built for Gretl is the product of a decidedly sensitive ear and <i>good</i> manners, and expression of great <i>understanding</i>... But <i>primordial</i> life, wild life striving to erupt into the open \xe2\x80\x93 that is lacking."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990240_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990240-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Monk comments that the same might be said of the technically excellent, but austere, terracotta sculpture Wittgenstein had modelled of Marguerite Respinger in 1926, and that, as Russell first noticed, this "wild life striving to be in the open" was precisely the substance of Wittgenstein\'s philosophical work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990240_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990240-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="1929.E2.80.931941:_Fellowship_at_Cambridge"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1929\xe2\x80\x931941:_Fellowship_at_Cambridge">1929\xe2\x80\x931941: Fellowship at Cambridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: 1929\xe2\x80\x931941: Fellowship at Cambridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="PhD_and_fellowship">PhD and fellowship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: PhD and fellowship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg/220px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg" width="220" height="322" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg/330px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg/440px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg 2x" data-file-width="547" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ludwig Wittgenstein, 1930</div></div></div>\n<p>According to Feigl (as reported by Monk), upon attending a conference in Vienna by mathematician Brouwer, Wittgenstein remained quite impressed, taking into consideration the possibility of a "return to Philosophy". At the urging of Ramsey and others, Wittgenstein returned to Cambridge in 1929. Keynes wrote in a letter to his wife: "Well, God has arrived. I met him on the 5.15 train."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990255_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990255-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> Despite this fame, he could not initially work at Cambridge as he did not have a degree, so he applied as an advanced undergraduate. Russell noted that his previous residency was sufficient to fulfill eligibility requirements for a PhD, and urged him to offer the <i>Tractatus</i> as his thesis.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It was examined in 1929 by Russell and Moore; at the end of the thesis defence, Wittgenstein clapped the two examiners on the shoulder and said, "Don\'t worry, I know you\'ll never understand it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990271_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990271-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> Moore wrote in the examiner\'s report: "I myself consider that this is a work of genius; but, even if I am completely mistaken and it is nothing of the sort, it is well above the standard required for the Ph.D. degree."<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein was appointed as a lecturer and was made a fellow of Trinity College.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anschluss">Anschluss</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Anschluss">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Laws" title="Nuremberg Laws">Nuremberg Laws</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mischling_Test" title="Mischling Test">Mischling Test</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:43._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_on_holiday.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/43._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_on_holiday.jpg/220px-43._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_on_holiday.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/43._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_on_holiday.jpg/330px-43._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_on_holiday.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/43._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_on_holiday.jpg/440px-43._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_on_holiday.jpg 2x" data-file-width="457" data-file-height="291" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:43._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_on_holiday.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein on holiday in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> with Gilbert Pattisson, July 1936</div></div></div><p>\nFrom 1936 to 1937, Wittgenstein lived again in Norway,<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> where he worked on the <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>. In the winter of 1936/7, he delivered a series of "confessions" to close friends, most of them about minor infractions like white lies, in an effort to cleanse himself. In 1938, he travelled to Ireland to visit <a href="/wiki/Maurice_O%27Connor_Drury" title="Maurice O&#39;Connor Drury">Maurice O\'Connor Drury</a>, a friend who became a psychiatrist, and considered such training himself, with the intention of abandoning philosophy for it. The visit to Ireland was at the same time a response to the invitation of the then Irish <a href="/wiki/Taoiseach" title="Taoiseach">Taoiseach</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89amon_de_Valera" title="\xc3\x89amon de Valera">\xc3\x89amon de Valera</a>, himself a former mathematics teacher. De Valera hoped Wittgenstein\'s presence would contribute to the <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Institute_for_Advanced_Studies" title="Dublin Institute for Advanced Studies">Dublin Institute for Advanced Studies</a> which he was soon to set up.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:130px;"><a href="/wiki/File:21._Photographs_showing_Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_house_in_Norway.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/21._Photographs_showing_Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_house_in_Norway.jpg/128px-21._Photographs_showing_Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_house_in_Norway.jpg" width="128" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/21._Photographs_showing_Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_house_in_Norway.jpg/193px-21._Photographs_showing_Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_house_in_Norway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d3/21._Photographs_showing_Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_house_in_Norway.jpg 2x" data-file-width="236" data-file-height="391" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:21._Photographs_showing_Wittgenstein%E2%80%99s_house_in_Norway.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photograph showing Wittgenstein\xe2\x80\x99s house in <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, sent by Wittgenstein to <a href="/wiki/G._E._Moore" title="G. E. Moore">G. E. Moore</a>, October 1936</div></div></div><p>While he was in Ireland in March 1938, Germany annexed Austria in the <i>Anschluss</i>; the Viennese Wittgenstein was now a citizen of the enlarged Germany and a Jew under the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Laws" title="Nuremberg Laws">1935 Nuremberg racial laws</a>, because three of his grandparents had been born as Jews. The Nuremberg Laws classified people as Jews (<i>Volljuden</i>) if they had three or four Jewish grandparents, and as mixed blood (<i><a href="/wiki/Mischling" title="Mischling">Mischling</a></i>) if they had one or two. It meant <i>inter alia</i> that the Wittgensteins were restricted in whom they could marry or have sex with, and where they could work.<sup id="cite_ref-Waugh137_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh137-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the Anschluss, his brother Paul left almost immediately for England, and later the US. The Nazis discovered his relationship with Hilde Schania, a brewer\'s daughter with whom he had had two children but whom he had never married, though he did later. Because she was not Jewish, he was served with a summons for <i><a href="/wiki/Rassenschande" title="Rassenschande">Rassenschande</a></i> (racial defilement). He told no one he was leaving the country, except for Hilde who agreed to follow him. He left so suddenly and quietly that for a time people believed he was the fourth Wittgenstein brother to have committed suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wittgenstein began to investigate acquiring British or Irish citizenship with the help of Keynes, and apparently had to confess to his friends in England that he had earlier misrepresented himself to them as having just one Jewish grandparent, when in fact he had three.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A few days before the invasion of Poland, Hitler personally granted <i>Mischling</i> status to the Wittgenstein siblings. In 1939 there were 2,100 applications for this, and Hitler granted only 12.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Anthony Gottlieb writes that the pretext was that their paternal grandfather had been the bastard son of a German prince, which allowed the Reichsbank to claim foreign currency, stocks and 1700&#160;kg of gold held in Switzerland by a Wittgenstein family trust. Gretl, an American citizen by marriage, started the negotiations over the racial status of their grandfather, and the family\'s large foreign currency reserves were used as a bargaining tool. Paul had escaped to Switzerland and then the US in July 1938, and disagreed with the negotiations, leading to a permanent split between the siblings. After the war, when Paul was performing in Vienna, he did not visit Hermine who was dying there, and he had no further contact with Ludwig or Gretl.<sup id="cite_ref-Gottlieb_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gottlieb-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Professor_of_philosophy">Professor of philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Professor of philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:46._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_the_Fellows%27_Garden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/46._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_the_Fellows%27_Garden.jpg/220px-46._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_the_Fellows%27_Garden.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/46._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_the_Fellows%27_Garden.jpg/330px-46._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_the_Fellows%27_Garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/46._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_the_Fellows%27_Garden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="407" data-file-height="330" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:46._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_the_Fellows%27_Garden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein in the Fellows\xe2\x80\x99 Garden at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity</a>, 1939</div></div></div>\n<p>After G. E. Moore resigned the chair in philosophy in 1939, Wittgenstein was elected, and acquired <a href="/wiki/British_subject" title="British subject">British citizenship</a> soon afterwards. In July 1939 he travelled to Vienna to assist Gretl and his other sisters, visiting Berlin for one day to meet an official of the Reichsbank. After this, he travelled to New York to persuade Paul, whose agreement was required, to back the scheme. The required Befreiung was granted in August 1939. The unknown amount signed over to the Nazis by the Wittgenstein family, a week or so before the outbreak of war, included amongst many other assets, 1,700&#160;kg of gold.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> There is a report Wittgenstein visited Moscow a second time in 1939, travelling from Berlin, and again met the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Sofya_Yanovskaya" title="Sofya Yanovskaya">Sophia Janowskaya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nNorman Malcolm, at the time a post-graduate research fellow at Cambridge, describes his first impressions of Wittgenstein in 1938: </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"At a meeting of the Moral Science Club, after the paper for the evening was read and the discussion started, someone began to stammer a remark. He had extreme difficulty in expressing himself and his words were unintelligible to me. I whispered to my neighbour, \'Who\'s that?\': he replied, \'Wittgenstein\'. I was astonished because I had expected the famous author of the <i>Tractatus</i> to be an elderly man, whereas this man looked <i>young</i> \xe2\x80\x93 perhaps about 35. (His actual age was 49.) His face was lean and brown, his profile was aquiline and strikingly beautiful, his head was covered with a curly mass of brown hair. I observed the respectful attention that everyone in the room paid to him. After this unsuccessful beginning he did not speak for a time but was obviously struggling with his thoughts. His look was concentrated, he made striking gestures with his hands as if he was discoursing... Whether lecturing or conversing privately, Wittgenstein always spoke emphatically and with a distinctive intonation. He spoke excellent English, with the accent of an educated Englishman, although occasional Germanisms would appear in his constructions. His voice was resonant... His words came out, not fluently, but with great force. Anyone who heard him say anything knew that this was a singular person. His face was remarkably mobile and expressive when he talked. His eyes were deep and often fierce in their expression. His whole personality was commanding, even imperial."<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote><p>\nDescribing Wittgenstein\'s lecture program, Malcolm continues: </p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"It is hardly correct to speak of these meetings as \'lectures\', although this is what Wittgenstein called them. For one thing, he was carrying on original research in these meetings... Often the meetings consisted mainly of dialogue. Sometimes, however, when he was trying to draw a thought out of himself, he would prohibit, with a peremptory motion of the hand, any questions or remarks. There were frequent and prolonged periods of silence, with only an occasional mutter from Wittgenstein, and the stillest attention from the others. During these silences, Wittgenstein was extremely tense and active. His gaze was concentrated; his face was alive; his hands made arresting movements; his expression was stern. One knew that one was in the presence of extreme seriousness, absorption, and force of intellect... Wittgenstein was a frightening person at these classes."<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>After work, the philosopher would often relax by watching <a href="/wiki/Western_Movie" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Movie">Westerns</a>, where he preferred to sit at the very front of the cinema, or reading detective stories especially the ones written by <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Davis" title="Norbert Davis">Norbert Davis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990528_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990528-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Norman Malcolm wrote that Wittgenstein would rush to the cinema when class ended.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By this time, Wittgenstein\'s view on the <a href="/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations of mathematics</a> had changed considerably. In his early 20s, Wittgenstein had thought logic could provide a solid foundation, and he had even considered updating Russell and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Whitehead</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Principia_Mathematica" title="Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i>. Now he denied there were any mathematical facts to be discovered. He gave a series of lectures on mathematics, discussing this and other topics, documented in a book, with lectures by Wittgenstein and discussions between him and several students, including the young <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a> who described Wittgenstein as "a <i>very</i> peculiar man". The two had many discussions about the relationship between computational logic and everyday notions of truth.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="1941.E2.80.931947:_World_War_II_and_Guy.27s_Hospital"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1941\xe2\x80\x931947:_World_War_II_and_Guy\'s_Hospital">1941\xe2\x80\x931947: World War II and Guy\'s Hospital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: 1941\xe2\x80\x931947: World War II and Guy&#039;s Hospital">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:179px;"><a href="/wiki/File:50._Wittgenstein_in_Swansea_(taken_by_Ben_Richards).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/50._Wittgenstein_in_Swansea_%28taken_by_Ben_Richards%29.jpg/177px-50._Wittgenstein_in_Swansea_%28taken_by_Ben_Richards%29.jpg" width="177" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/50._Wittgenstein_in_Swansea_%28taken_by_Ben_Richards%29.jpg/265px-50._Wittgenstein_in_Swansea_%28taken_by_Ben_Richards%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/50._Wittgenstein_in_Swansea_%28taken_by_Ben_Richards%29.jpg/353px-50._Wittgenstein_in_Swansea_%28taken_by_Ben_Richards%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="372" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:50._Wittgenstein_in_Swansea_(taken_by_Ben_Richards).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein in <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a>, Summer 1947</div></div></div>\n<p>Monk writes that Wittgenstein found it intolerable that a war was going on and he was teaching philosophy. He grew angry when any of his students wanted to become professional philosophers.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In September 1941, he asked <a href="/wiki/John_Ryle_(professor)" class="mw-redirect" title="John Ryle (professor)">John Ryle</a>, the brother of the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Ryle" title="Gilbert Ryle">Gilbert Ryle</a>, if he could get a manual job at <a href="/wiki/Guy%27s_Hospital" title="Guy&#39;s Hospital">Guy\'s Hospital</a> in London. John Ryle was professor of medicine at Cambridge and had been involved in helping Guy\'s prepare for <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">the Blitz</a>. Wittgenstein told Ryle he would die slowly if left at Cambridge, and he would rather die quickly. He started working at Guy\'s shortly afterwards as a dispensary porter, delivering drugs from the pharmacy to the wards where he apparently advised the patients not to take them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990431_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990431-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn the new year of 1942, Ryle took Wittgenstein to his home in Sussex to meet his wife who had been adamant to meet him. Ryle\'s son recorded the weekend in his diary;</p><blockquote><p>Wink is awful strange \xe2\x80\x93 not a very good english speaker, keeps on saying \xe2\x80\x98I mean\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98its \xe2\x80\x9ctolerable\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99 meaning intolerable.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990434_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990434-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> </p></blockquote><p>The hospital staff were not told he was one of the world\'s most famous philosophers, though some of the medical staff did recognize him\xe2\x80\x94at least one had attended Moral Sciences Club meetings\xe2\x80\x94but they were discreet. "Good God, don\'t tell anybody who I am!" Wittgenstein begged one of them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990432_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990432-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Some of them nevertheless called him Professor Wittgenstein, and he was allowed to dine with the doctors. He wrote on 1 April 1942: "I no longer feel any hope for the future of my life. It is as though I had before me nothing more than a long stretch of living death. I cannot imagine any future for me other than a ghastly one.
