Friendless and joyless."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990431_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990431-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> It was at this time that Wittgenstein had an operation at Guy\'s to remove a gall-stone that had troubled him for some years.\n</p><p>He had developed a friendship with Keith Kirk, a working-class teenage friend of <a href="/wiki/Francis_Skinner" title="Francis Skinner">Francis Skinner</a>, the mathematics undergraduate he had had a relationship with until Skinner\'s death in 1941 from <a href="/wiki/Polio" class="mw-redirect" title="Polio">polio</a>. Skinner had given up academia, thanks at least in part to Wittgenstein\'s influence, and had been working as a mechanic in 1939, with Kirk as his apprentice. Kirk and Wittgenstein struck up a friendship, with Wittgenstein giving him lessons in physics to help him pass a <a href="/wiki/City_and_Guilds" class="mw-redirect" title="City and Guilds">City and Guilds</a> exam. During his period of loneliness at Guy\'s he wrote in his diary: "For ten days I\'ve heard nothing more from K, even though I pressed him a week ago for news. I think that he has perhaps broken with me. A <i>tragic</i> thought!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990443_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990443-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Kirk had in fact got married, and they never saw one another again.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990442-423_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990442-423-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While Wittgenstein was at Guy\'s he met Basil Reeve, a young doctor with an interest in philosophy, who, with R. T. Grant, was studying the effect of shock on air-raid casualties. When the blitz ended there were fewer casualties to study. In November 1942, Grant and Reeve moved to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Victoria_Infirmary" title="Royal Victoria Infirmary">Royal Victoria Infirmary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle upon Tyne</a>, to study road traffic and industrial casualties. Grant offered Wittgenstein a position as a laboratory assistant at a wage of \xc2\xa34 per week, and he lived in Newcastle (at 28 Brandling Park, <a href="/wiki/Jesmond" title="Jesmond">Jesmond</a><sup id="cite_ref-Schardt_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schardt-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>) from 29 April 1943 until February 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990447_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990447-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the summer of 1946, Wittgenstein thought often of leaving Cambridge and resigning his position as Chair. Wittgenstein grew further dismayed at the state of philosophy, particularly about articles published in the journal <a href="/wiki/Mind_(journal)" title="Mind (journal)"><i>Mind</i></a>. It was around this time that Wittgenstein fell in love with Ben Richards writing in his diary, "The only thing that my love for B. has done for me is this: it has driven the other small worries associated with my position and my work into the background." On the 30th of September, Wittgenstein wrote about Cambridge after his return from Swansea, "Everything about the place repels me. The stiffness, the artificiality, the self-satisfaction of the people. The university atmosphere nauseates me."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990493_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990493-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wittgenstein had only maintained contact with Fouracre from Guy\'s hospital who had joined the army in 1943 after his marriage, only returning in 1947. Wittgenstein maintained frequent correspondence with Fouracre during his time away displaying a desire for Fouracre to return home urgently from the war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990493_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990493-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn May 1947, Wittgenstein addressed a group of Oxford philosophers for the first time at the Jowett Society. The discussion was on the validity of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren\xc3\xa9 Descartes">Descartes\'</a> <a href="/wiki/Cogito_ergo_sum" class="mw-redirect" title="Cogito ergo sum">Cogito ergo sum</a> where Wittgenstein ignored the question and applied his own philosophical method. <a href="/wiki/Harold_Arthur_Prichard" title="Harold Arthur Prichard">Harold Arthur Prichard</a> who attended the event was not pleased with Wittgenstein\'s methods;</p><blockquote><p>Wittgenstein: If a man says to me, looking at the sky, \xe2\x80\x98I think it will rain, therefore I exist,\xe2\x80\x99 I do not understand him.</p></blockquote><blockquote><p>Prichard: That\xe2\x80\x99s all very fine; what we want to know is: is the cogito valid or not?<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990496_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990496-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span id="1947.E2.80.931951:_Final_years"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1947\xe2\x80\x931951:_Final_years">1947\xe2\x80\x931951: Final years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: 1947\xe2\x80\x931951: Final years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="rquote pullquote floatleft" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:left; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">\n\n<tbody><tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">\n<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="padding:0 10px;">Death is not an event in life: we do not live to experience death. If we take eternity to mean not infinite temporal duration but timelessness, then eternal life belongs to those who live in the present. Our life has no end in the way in which our visual field has no limits.\n</td>\n<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:normal; text-align:right;"><cite>\xe2\x80\x94&#160;Wittgenstein, Tractatus, 6.431</cite>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:52._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_von_Wright%27s_garden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/52._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_von_Wright%27s_garden.jpg/220px-52._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_von_Wright%27s_garden.jpg" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/52._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_von_Wright%27s_garden.jpg/330px-52._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_von_Wright%27s_garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/52._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_von_Wright%27s_garden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="397" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:52._Ludwig_Wittgenstein_in_von_Wright%27s_garden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the last photographs taken of Wittgenstein, in the garden of <a href="/wiki/Georg_Henrik_von_Wright" title="Georg Henrik von Wright">Georg Henrik von Wrights\'</a> home in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a>, Summer 1950; Wittgenstein had taken the sheet from his bed and draped it behind him</div></div></div>\n<p>Wittgenstein resigned the professorship at Cambridge in 1947 to concentrate on his writing, and in 1947 and 1948 travelled to <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, staying at Ross\'s Hotel in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a> and at a farmhouse in <a href="/wiki/Redcross" title="Redcross">Redcross</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Wicklow" title="County Wicklow">County Wicklow</a>, where he began the manuscript volume MS 137, Band R. Seeking solitude he moved to <i>Rosro</i>, a holiday cottage in <a href="/wiki/Connemara" title="Connemara">Connemara</a> owned by Maurice O\'Connor-Drury.<sup id="cite_ref-archivefinalyears_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archivefinalyears-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He also accepted an invitation from <a href="/wiki/Norman_Malcolm" title="Norman Malcolm">Norman Malcolm</a>, then professor at Cornell University, to stay with him and his wife for several months at <a href="/wiki/Ithaca,_New_York" title="Ithaca, New York">Ithaca, New York</a>. He made the trip in April 1949, although he told Malcolm he was too unwell to do philosophical work: "I haven\'t done any work since the beginning of March &amp; I haven\'t had the strength of even trying to do any." A doctor in Dublin had diagnosed anaemia and prescribed iron and liver pills. The details of Wittgenstein\'s stay in America are recounted in Norman Malcolm\'s <i>Ludwig Wittgenstein: A Memoir</i>. During his summer in America, Wittgenstein began his epistemological discussions, in particular his engagement with <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">philosophical skepticism</a>, that would eventually become the final fragments <i><a href="/wiki/On_Certainty" title="On Certainty">On Certainty</a></i>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque,_76_Storey%27s_Way,_Cambridge,_UK.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg/220px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg/330px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg/440px-Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque%2C_76_Storey%27s_Way%2C_Cambridge%2C_UK.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Wittgenstein_Blue_Plaque,_76_Storey%27s_Way,_Cambridge,_UK.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The plaque at "Storey\'s End", 76 <a href="/wiki/Storey%27s_Way" title="Storey&#39;s Way">Storey\'s Way</a>, Cambridge, where Wittgenstein died.</div></div></div>\n<p>He returned to London, where he was diagnosed with an inoperable <a href="/wiki/Prostate_cancer" title="Prostate cancer">prostate cancer</a>, which had spread to his bone marrow. He spent the next two months in Vienna, where his sister Hermine died on 11 February 1950; he went to see her every day, but she was hardly able to speak or recognize him. "Great loss for me and all of us," he wrote. "Greater than I would have thought." He moved around a lot after Hermine\'s death staying with various friends: to Cambridge in April 1950, where he stayed with <a href="/wiki/Georg_Henrik_von_Wright" title="Georg Henrik von Wright">G. H. von Wright</a>; to London to stay with <a href="/wiki/Rush_Rhees" title="Rush Rhees">Rush Rhees</a>; then to Oxford to see <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Anscombe" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth Anscombe">Elizabeth Anscombe</a>, writing to Norman Malcolm that he was hardly doing any philosophy. He went to Norway in August with Ben Richards, then returned to Cambridge, where on 27 November he moved into <i>Storey\'s End</i> at 76 <a href="/wiki/Storey%27s_Way" title="Storey&#39;s Way">Storey\'s Way</a>, the home of his doctor, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Vaughan_Bevan" title="Edward Vaughan Bevan">Edward Bevan</a>, and his wife Joan; he had told them he did not want to die in a hospital, so they said he could spend his last days in their home instead. Joan at first was afraid of Wittgenstein, but they soon became good friends.<sup id="cite_ref-archivefinalyears_209-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archivefinalyears-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the beginning of 1951, it was clear that he had little time left. He wrote a new will in Oxford on 29 January, naming Rhees as his executor, and Anscombe and von Wright his literary administrators, and wrote to Norman Malcolm that month to say, "My mind\'s completely dead. This isn\'t a complaint, for I don\'t really suffer from it. I know that life must have an end once and that mental life can cease before the rest does."<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> In February he returned to the Bevans\' home to work on MS 175 and MS 176. These and other manuscripts were later published as <i><a href="/wiki/Remarks_on_Colour" title="Remarks on Colour">Remarks on Colour</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/On_Certainty" title="On Certainty">On Certainty</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-archivefinalyears_209-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archivefinalyears-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> He wrote to Malcolm on 16 April 13 days before his death: "An extraordinary thing happened to me. About a month ago I suddenly found myself in the right frame of mind for doing philosophy. I had been <i>absolutely</i> certain that I\'d never again be able to do it. It\'s the first time after more than 2 years that the curtain in my brain has gone up.\xe2\x80\x94Of course, so far I\'ve only worked for about 5 weeks &amp; it may be all over by tomorrow; but it bucks me up a lot now."<sup id="cite_ref-Malcolm80_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malcolm80-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Death_bed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Death_bed.jpg/220px-Death_bed.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Death_bed.jpg/330px-Death_bed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Death_bed.jpg/440px-Death_bed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Death_bed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein on his deathbed, 1951</div></div></div>\n<p>Wittgenstein began work on his final manuscript, MS 177, on 25 April 1951. It was his 62nd birthday on 26 April. He went for a walk the next afternoon, and wrote his last entry that day, 27 April. That evening, he became very ill; when his doctor told him he might live only a few days, he reportedly replied, "Good!" Joan stayed with him throughout that night, and just before losing consciousness for the last time on 28 April, he told her: "Tell them I\'ve had a wonderful life." Norman Malcolm describes this as a "...strangely moving utterance."<sup id="cite_ref-Malcolm80_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Malcolm80-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="rquote pullquote floatleft" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:left; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">\n\n<tbody><tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">\n<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">\xe2\x80\x9c\n</td>\n<td style="padding:0 10px;"><i>I won\'t say \'See you tomorrow\' because that would be like predicting the future, and I\'m pretty sure I can\'t do that.</i>\n</td>\n<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:&#39;Times New Roman&#39;,serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">\xe2\x80\x9d\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:normal; text-align:right;"><cite>\xe2\x80\x94&#160;Wittgenstein, 1949<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup></cite>\n</td></tr></tbody></table><p>\nFour of Wittgenstein\'s former students arrived at his bedside\xe2\x80\x94Ben Richards, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Anscombe" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth Anscombe">Elizabeth Anscombe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yorick_Smythies" title="Yorick Smythies">Yorick Smythies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_O%27Connor_Drury" title="Maurice O&#39;Connor Drury">Maurice O\'Connor Drury</a>. Anscombe and Smythies were Catholics; and, at the latter\'s request, a Dominican friar, Father <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Pepler" title="Conrad Pepler">Conrad Pepler</a>, also attended. They were at first unsure what Wittgenstein would have wanted, but then remembered he had said he hoped his Catholic friends would pray for him, so they did, and he was pronounced dead shortly afterwards.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg/220px-Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg/330px-Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg/440px-Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1296" data-file-height="972" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wittgenstein_Gravestone.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wittgenstein\'s grave at the <a href="/wiki/Ascension_Parish_Burial_Ground,_Cambridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascension Parish Burial Ground, Cambridge">Ascension Parish Burial Ground</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Wittgenstein was given a Catholic burial at <a href="/wiki/Ascension_Parish_Burial_Ground,_Cambridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascension Parish Burial Ground, Cambridge">Parish of the Ascension Burial Ground</a> in Cambridge.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Drury later said he had been troubled ever since about whether that was the right thing to do.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990576-580_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990576-580-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> In 2015 the ledger gravestone was refurbished by the British Wittgenstein Society.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On his religious views, Wittgenstein was said to be greatly interested in <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> and was sympathetic to it. In his university years, he expressed belief in the <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrection of Jesus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-res_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-res-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> However, he did not consider himself to be a Catholic. According to <a href="/wiki/Norman_Malcolm" title="Norman Malcolm">Norman Malcolm</a>, Wittgenstein saw Catholicism as more of a way of life than as a set of beliefs he personally held, considering that he did not accept any religious faith.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wittgenstein was said by some commentators to be <a href="/wiki/Agnostic" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnostic">agnostic</a>, in a qualified sense.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1953:_Publication_of_the_Philosophical_Investigations">1953: Publication of the <i>Philosophical Investigations</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: 1953: Publication of the Philosophical Investigations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Investigations" title="Philosophical Investigations">Philosophical Investigations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language-game" class="mw-redirect" title="Language-game">Language-game</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Private_language_argument" title="Private language argument">Private language argument</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaninchen_und_Ente.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Kaninchen_und_Ente.png/220px-Kaninchen_und_Ente.png" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Kaninchen_und_Ente.png/330px-Kaninchen_und_Ente.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Kaninchen_und_Ente.png/440px-Kaninchen_und_Ente.png 2x" data-file-width="1414" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaninchen_und_Ente.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of a "<a href="/wiki/Rabbit%E2%80%93duck_illusion" title="Rabbit\xe2\x80\x93duck illusion">duckrabbit</a>", discussed in the <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>, section XI, part II</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Blue_and_Brown_Books" title="Blue and Brown Books">Blue Book</a></i>, a set of notes dictated to his class at Cambridge in 1933\xe2\x80\x931934, contains the seeds of Wittgenstein\'s later thoughts on language and is widely read as a turning-point in his philosophy of language.\n</p><p><i>Philosophical Investigations</i> was published in two parts in 1953. Most of Part I was ready for printing in 1946, but Wittgenstein withdrew the manuscript from his publisher. The shorter Part II was added by his editors, Elizabeth Anscombe and Rush Rhees. Wittgenstein asks the reader to think of language as a multiplicity of language-games within which parts of language develop and function. He argues the bewitchments of philosophical problems arise from philosophers\' misguided attempts to consider the meaning of words independently of their context, usage, and grammar, what he called "language gone on holiday."<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to Wittgenstein, philosophical problems arise when language is forced from its proper home into a metaphysical environment, where all the familiar and necessary landmarks and contextual clues are removed. He describes this metaphysical environment as like being on frictionless ice: where the conditions are apparently perfect for a philosophically and logically perfect language, all philosophical problems can be solved without the muddying effects of everyday contexts; but where, precisely because of the lack of friction, language can in fact do no work at all.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> Wittgenstein argues that philosophers must leave the frictionless ice and return to the "rough ground" of ordinary language in use. Much of the <i>Investigations</i> consists of examples of how the first false steps can be avoided, so that philosophical problems are dissolved, rather than solved: "the clarity we are aiming at is indeed <i>complete</i> clarity. But this simply means that the philosophical problems should <i>completely</i> disappear."<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Wittgenstein left a voluminous archive of unpublished papers, including 83 manuscripts, 46 typescripts and 11 dictations, amounting to an estimated 20,000 pages. Choosing among repeated drafts, revisions, corrections and loose notes editorial work has found nearly one third of the total suitable for print.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> An Internet facility hosted by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bergen" title="University of Bergen">University of Bergen</a> allows access to images of almost all the material and to search the available transcriptions.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> In 2011 two new boxes of Wittgenstein papers were found.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Investigations" title="Philosophical Investigations">Philosophical Investigations</a></i> was the only nearly finished project and the book was published in 1953.\n</p><p>In 1999 a survey among American university and college teachers ranked the <i>Investigations</i> as the most important book of 20th-century philosophy, standing out as "the one crossover masterpiece in twentieth-century philosophy, appealing across diverse specializations and philosophical orientations."<sup id="cite_ref-here_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-here-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The <i>Investigations</i> also ranked 54th on a list of most influential twentieth-century works in cognitive science prepared by the University of Minnesota\'s Center for Cognitive Sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-milproj_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-milproj-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Peter_Hacker" title="Peter Hacker">Peter Hacker</a> argues that Wittgenstein\'s influence on 20th-century analytical philosophy can be attributed to his early influence on the Vienna Circle and later influence on the Oxford "ordinary language" school and Cambridge philosophers.<sup id="cite_ref-hacker_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hacker-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nWittgenstein\'s influence has been felt in nearly every field of the humanities and social sciences, yet there are diverging interpretations of his thought. In the words of his friend and colleague <a href="/wiki/Georg_Henrik_von_Wright" title="Georg Henrik von Wright">Georg Henrik von Wright</a>: </p><blockquote><p>He was of the opinion... that his ideas were generally misunderstood and distorted even by those who professed to be his disciples. He doubted he would be better understood in the future. He once said he felt as though he was writing for people who would think in a different way, breathe a different air of life, from that of present-day men.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interpreters">Interpreters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Interpreters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Since Wittgenstein\'s death, scholarly interpretations of his philosophy have differed. Scholars have differed on the continuity between "early" and "late" Wittgenstein (that is, the difference between his views expressed in the <i>Tractatus</i> and those in <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>), with some seeing the two as starkly disparate and others stressing the gradual transition between the two works through analysis of Wittgenstein\'s unpublished papers (the <i><a href="/wiki/Nachlass" title="Nachlass">Nachlass</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Glock_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glock-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One significant debate in Wittgenstein scholarship concerns the work of interpreters who are referred to under the banner of <a href="/wiki/The_New_Wittgenstein" title="The New Wittgenstein">The New Wittgenstein</a> school such as <a href="/wiki/Cora_Diamond" title="Cora Diamond">Cora Diamond</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alice_Crary" title="Alice Crary">Alice Crary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_F._Conant" title="James F. Conant">James F. Conant</a>.  While the <i>Tractatus</i>, particularly in its conclusion, seems paradoxical and self-undermining, New Wittgenstein scholars advance a "therapeutic" understanding of Wittgenstein\'s work\xe2\x80\x94"an understanding of Wittgenstein as aspiring, not to advance metaphysical theories, but rather to help us work ourselves out of confusions we become entangled in when philosophizing."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrary20001_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrary20001-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> To support this goal, the New Wittgenstein scholars propose a reading of the <i>Tractatus</i> as "plain nonsense"\xe2\x80\x94arguing it does not attempt to convey a substantive philosophical project but instead simply tries to push the reader to abandon philosophical speculation. The therapeutic approach traces its roots to the philosophical work of <a href="/wiki/John_Wisdom" title="John Wisdom">John Wisdom</a><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> and the review of <i>The Blue Book</i> written by <a href="/wiki/Oets_Kolk_Bouwsma" title="Oets Kolk Bouwsma">Oets Kolk Bouwsma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Glock_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glock-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The therapeutic approach is not without critics: <a href="/wiki/Hans-Johann_Glock" title="Hans-Johann Glock">Hans-Johann Glock</a> argues that the "plain nonsense" reading of the <i>Tractatus</i> "...is at odds with the external evidence, writings and conversations in which Wittgenstein states that the <i>Tractatus</i> is committed to the idea of ineffable insight."<sup id="cite_ref-Glock_228-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glock-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bertrand_Russell">Bertrand Russell</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Bertrand Russell">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4><p>\nIn October 1944, Wittgenstein returned to Cambridge around the same time as Russell who had been living in <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">America</a> for several years. Russell returned to Cambridge after a backlash in America to his writings on morals and religion. Wittgenstein said of Russell\'s works to Drury;</p><blockquote><p>Russell\xe2\x80\x99s books should be bound in two colours\xe2\x80\xa6those dealing with mathematical logic in red \xe2\x80\x93 and all students of philosophy should read them; those dealing with ethics and politics in blue \xe2\x80\x93 and no one should be allowed to read them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMonk1990471_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMonk1990471-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Russell made similar disparaging comments about Wittgenstein\'s later work;</p><blockquote><p>I have not found in Wittgenstein\'s Philosophical Investigations anything that seemed to me interesting and I do not understand why a whole school finds important wisdom in its pages. Psychologically this is surprising. The earlier Wittgenstein, whom I knew intimately, was a man addicted to passionately intense thinking, profoundly aware of difficult problems of which I, like him, felt the importance, and possessed (or at least so I thought) of true philosophical genius. The later Wittgenstein, on the contrary, seems to have grown tired of serious thinking and to have invented a doctrine which would make such an activity unnecessary. I do not for one moment believe that the doctrine which has these lazy consequences is true. I realize, however, that I have an overpoweringly strong bias against it, for, if it is true, philosophy is, at best, a slight help to lexicographers, and at worst, an idle tea-table amusement.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Saul_Kripke">Saul Kripke</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Saul Kripke">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Saul_Kripke" title="Saul Kripke">Saul Kripke</a>\'s 1982 book <i><a href="/wiki/Wittgenstein_on_Rules_and_Private_Language" title="Wittgenstein on Rules and Private Language">Wittgenstein on Rules and Private Language</a></i> contends that the central argument of <a href="/wiki/Wittgenstein" class="mw-redirect" title="Wittgenstein">Wittgenstein</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Investigations" title="Philosophical Investigations">Philosophical Investigations</a></i> centers on a devastating <a href="/wiki/Rule-following_paradox" class="mw-redirect" title="Rule-following paradox">rule-following paradox</a> that undermines the possibility of our ever following rules in our use of language.  Kripke writes that this paradox is "the most radical and original skeptical problem that philosophy has seen to date."<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Kripke\'s book generated a large secondary literature, divided between those who find his skeptical problem interesting and perceptive, and others, such as <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Baker" class="mw-redirect" title="Gordon Baker">Gordon Baker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hacker" title="Peter Hacker">Peter Hacker</a>, who argue that his meaning skepticism is a pseudo-problem that stems from a confused, selective reading of Wittgenstein. Kripke\'s position has, however recently been defended against these and other attacks by the Cambridge philosopher <a href="/wiki/Martin_Kusch" title="Martin Kusch">Martin Kusch</a> (2006), and Wittgenstein scholar <a href="/w/index.php?title=David_G._Stern&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="David G. Stern (page does not exist)">David G. Stern</a> considers the book to be "the most influential and widely discussed" work on Wittgenstein since the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853264625">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{list-style-type:none;margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>dl>dd{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-100{font-size:100%}</style><div class="refbegin columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">\n<p>A collection of Ludwig Wittgenstein\'s manuscripts is held by Trinity College, Cambridge.\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Logisch-Philosophische Abhandlung</i>, Annalen der Naturphilosophie, 14 (1921)\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Logico-Philosophicus" title="Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus">Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus</a></i> [<i>TLP</i>], translated by C.K. Ogden (1922)</li></ul></li>\n<li><i>Philosophische Untersuchungen</i> (1953)\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Investigations" title="Philosophical Investigations">Philosophical Investigations</a></i> [<i>PI</i>], translated by G.E.M. Anscombe (1953)</li></ul></li>\n<li><i>Bemerkungen \xc3\xbcber die Grundlagen der Mathematik</i>, ed. by G.H. von Wright, R. Rhees, and G.E.M. Anscombe (1956), a selection of his work on the philosophy of logic and mathematics between 1937 and 1944.\n<ul><li><i>Remarks on the Foundations of Mathematics</i>, translated by G.E.M. Anscombe, rev. ed. (1978)</li></ul></li>\n<li><i>Bemerkungen \xc3\xbcber die Philosophie der Psychologie</i>, ed. G.E.M. Anscombe and G.H. von Wright (1980)\n<ul><li><i>Remarks on the Philosophy of Psychology, Vols. 1 and 2</i>, translated by G.E.M. Anscombe, ed. G.E.M. Anscombe and G.H. von Wright (1980), a selection of which makes up <i>Zettel</i>.</li></ul></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Blue_and_Brown_Books" title="Blue and Brown Books">Blue and Brown Books</a></i> (1958), notes dictated in English to Cambridge students in 1933\xe2\x80\x931935.</li>\n<li><i>Philosophische Bemerkungen</i>, ed. by Rush Rhees (1964)\n<ul><li><i>Philosophical Remarks</i> (1975)</li>\n<li><i>Philosophical Grammar</i> (1978)</li></ul></li>\n<li><i>Bemerkungen \xc3\xbcber die Farben</i>, ed. by G.E.M. Anscombe (1977)\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Remarks_on_Colour" title="Remarks on Colour">Remarks on Colour</a></i> (1991), remarks on <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Theory_of_Colours" title="Theory of Colours">Theory of Colours</a></i>.</li></ul></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/On_Certainty" title="On Certainty">On Certainty</a></i>, collection of aphorisms discussing the relation between knowledge and certainty, extremely influential in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_action" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophy of action">philosophy of action</a>.</li>\n<li><i>Culture and Value</i>, collection of personal remarks about various cultural issues, such as religion and music, as well as critique of <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="S\xc3\xb8ren Kierkegaard">S\xc3\xb8ren Kierkegaard</a>\'s philosophy.</li>\n<li><i>Zettel</i>, collection of Wittgenstein\'s thoughts in fragmentary/"diary entry" format as with <i>On Certainty</i> and <i>Culture and Value</i>.</li></ul>\n<dl><dt>Works online</dt></dl>\n<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nlx.com/collections/166"><i>Wittgenstein: Gesamtbriefwechsel/Complete Correspondence</i>. Innsbrucker Electronic Edition</a>: <i>Ludwig Wittgenstein: Gesamtbriefwechsel/Complete Correspondence</i> contains Wittgenstein\'s collected correspondence, edited under the auspices of the Brenner-Archiv\'s Research Institute (University of Innsbruck). Editors (first edition): Monika Seekircher, Brian McGuinness and Anton Unterkircher. Editors (second edition): Anna Coda, Gabriel Citron, Barbara Halder, Allan Janik, Ulrich Lobis, Kerstin Mayr, Brian McGuinness, Michael Schorner, Monika Seekircher and Joseph Wang.</li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nlx.com/collections/124"><i>Wittgensteins Nachlass</i>. The Bergen Electronic Edition</a>: The collection includes all of Wittgenstein\'s unpublished manuscripts, typescripts, dictations, and most of his notebooks. The Nachlass was catalogued by G. H. von Wright in his <i>The Wittgenstein Papers</i>, first published in 1969, and later updated and included as a chapter with the same title in his book Wittgenstein, published by Blackwell (and by the University of Minnesota Press in the U.S.) in 1982.</li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://fair-use.org/the-cambridge-review/1913/03/06/reviews/the-science-of-logic">Review of P. Coffey\'s <i>Science of Logic</i></a> (1913): a polemical book review, written in 1912 for the March 1913 issue of <i>The Cambridge Review</i> when Wittgenstein was an undergraduate studying with Russell. The review is the earliest public record of Wittgenstein\'s philosophical views.</li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://wittgensteinsource.org/">Wittgenstein Source: 5 000 pages of the Wittgenstein Nachlass online</a></li>\n<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.gutenberg.org/author/Ludwig+Wittgenstein">Works by Ludwig Wittgenstein</a> at <a 10 December 2005)</li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span The archives give the date of the image as circa 1901.</li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-82"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-82">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thiede, Roger. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.focus.de/auto/neuheiten/zeitgeschichte-phantom-wittgenstein_aid_169829.html">"Phantom Wittgenstein"</a>, <i>Focus</i> magazine, 16 March 1998.\n<ul><li>The German Federal Archives says the image was taken "circa 1901"; it identifies the class as 1B and the teacher as Oskar Langer. See the full image and description at the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.bild.bundesarchiv.de/cross-search/search/_1283821026/">Bundesarchiv</a>. Retrieved 6 September 2010. The archive gives the date as circa 1901, but wrongly calls it the Realschule in Leonding, near Linz. Hitler attended primary school in Leonding, but from September 1901 went to the Realschule in Linz itself. See Kershaw, Ian. <i>Hitler, 1889\xe2\x80\x931936</i>. W. W. Norton &amp; Company, 2000, p. 16ff.</li>\n<li>Christoph Haidacher and Richard Schober write that Langer taught at the school from 1884 until 1901; see Haidacher, Christoph and Schober, Richard. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?ei=4tyFTJ-CLtGknQfJqOHhAQ&amp;ct=result&amp;id=XqQUAQAAIAAJ&amp;dq=%22Oskar+langer%22+hitler&amp;q=%22Oskar+langer%22#search_anchor"><i>Von Stadtstaaten und Imperien</i></a>, Universit\xc3\xa4tsverlag Wagner, 2006, p. 140.</li></ul>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See, e.g., MS 154.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Culture and Value</i>, Ludwig Wittgenstein, (Oxford 1998), page 16e (see also, pages 15e\xe2\x80\x9319e).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">M.O\'C. Retrieved 4 September 2010.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, Ramsey translated "<i>Sachverhalt</i>" and "<i>Sachlage</i>" as "atomic fact" and "state of affairs" respectively. But Wittgenstein discusses non-existent "<i>Sachverhalten</i>", and there cannot be a non-existent fact. Pears and McGuinness made a number of changes, including translating "<i>Sachverhalt</i>" as "state of affairs" and "<i>Sachlage</i>" as <i>situation</i>. The new translation is often preferred, but some philosophers use the original, in part because Wittgenstein approved it, and because it avoids the idiomatic English of Pears-McGuinness. See:\n<ul><li>White, Roger. <i>Wittgenstein\'s Tractatus logico-philosophicus</i>. Continuum International Publishing Group, 2006, p. 145.</li>\n<li>For a discussion about the relative merits of the translations, see Morris, Michael Rowland. "Introduction", <i>Routledge philosophy guidebook to Wittgenstein and the Tractatus</i>. Taylor &amp; Francis, 2008; and Nelson, John O. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/1467-9205.00092/abstract">"Is the Pears-McGuinness translation of the Tractatus really superior to Ogden\'s and Ramsey\'s?"</a>, <i>Philosophical Investigations</i>, 22:2, April 1999.</li>\n<li>See the three versions (Wittgenstein\'s German, published 1921; Ramsey-Ogden\'s translation, published 1922; and the Pears-McGuinness translation, published 1961) side by side <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://people.umass.edu/phil335-klement-2/tlp/tlp.html#bodytext">here</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110607070125/http://people.umass.edu/phil335-klement-2/tlp/tlp.html">Archived</a> 7 June 2011 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">16 February</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=www.britishwittgensteinsociety.org&amp;rft.atitle=Wittgenstein%27s+Grave+-&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.britishwittgensteinsociety.org%2Fwittgensteins-grave&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALudwig+Wittgenstein" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-res-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-res_216-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFFinch1984" class="citation">Finch, Peter (1984), <i>Ludwig Wittgenstein, Culture and Value</i>, Chicago: University of Chicago Press, p.&#160;33, <q>What inclines even me to believe in Christ\'s resurrection? I play as it were with the thought.\xe2\x80\x93If he did not rise from the dead, then he decomposed in the grave like every human being. He is dead &amp; decomposed. In that case he is a teacher, like any other &amp; can no longer help; &amp; we are once more orphaned &amp; alone. And have to make do with wisdom &amp; speculation. It is as though we are in a hell, where we can only dream &amp; are shut out from heaven, roofed in as it were. But if I am to be REALLY redeemed,\xe2\x80\x93 I need certainty\xe2\x80\x93not wisdom, dreams, speculation\xe2\x80\x93and this certainty is faith. And faith is faith in what my heart, my soul, needs, not my speculative intellect. For my soul, with its passions, as it were with its flesh and blood, must be redeemed, not my abstract mind. Perhaps one may say: Only love can believe the Resurrection. Or: it is love that believes the Resurrection</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Ludwig+Wittgenstein%2C+Culture+and+Value&amp;rft.place=Chicago&amp;rft.pages=33&amp;rft.pub=University+of+Chicago+Press&amp;rft.date=1984&amp;rft.aulast=Finch&amp;rft.aufirst=Peter&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALudwig+Wittgenstein" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-217">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Norman Malcolm; G. H. Von Wright (2001). <i>Ludwig Wittgenstein: A Memoir</i>. <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a>. pp.&#160;59\xe2\x80\x9360. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780199247592" title="Special:BookSources/9780199247592">9780199247592</a>. <q>I believe that Wittgenstein was prepared by his own character and experience to comprehend the idea of a judging and redeeming God. But any cosmological conception of a Deity, derived from the notions of cause or of infinity, would be repugnant to him. He was impatient with \'proofs\' of the existence of God, and with attempts to give religion a rational foundation. ...I do not wish to give the impression that Wittgenstein accepted any religious faith\xe2\x80\x94he certainly did not\xe2\x80\x94or that he was a religious person. But I think that there was in him, in some sense, the possibility of religion. I believe that he looked on religion as a \'form of life\' (to use an expression from the Investigations) in which he did not participate, but with which he was sympathetic and which greatly interested him. Those who did participate he respected \xe2\x80\x94 although here as elsewhere he had contempt for insincerity. I suspect that he regarded religious belief as based on qualities of character and will that he himself did not possess. Of Smythies and Anscombe, both of whom had become Roman Catholics, he once said to me: \'I could not possibly bring myself to believe all the things that they believe.\' I think that in this remark he was not disparaging their belief. It was rather an observation about his own capacity.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Ludwig+Wittgenstein%3A+A+Memoir&amp;rft.pages=59-60&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.isbn=9780199247592&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALudwig+Wittgenstein" class="Z3988"></span><span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; his only interest is to keep discussions, whether religious or not, clear.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Wittgenstein%27s+Religious+Point+of+View&amp;rft.pages=47&amp;rft.pub=Continuum+International+Publishing+Group&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=9780826490278&amp;rft.au=Tim+Labron&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALudwig+Wittgenstein" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-219">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">William Child (2011). <i>Wittgenstein</i>. Taylor &amp; Francis. p.&#160;218. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781136731372" title="Special:BookSources/9781136731372">9781136731372</a>. <q>"Was Wittgenstein religious? If we call him an agnostic, this must not be understood in the sense of the familiar polemical agnosticism that concentrates, and prides itself, on the argument that man could never know about these matters. The idea of a God in the sense of the Bible, the image of God as the creator of the world, hardly ever engaged Wittgenstein\'s attention..., but the notion of a <a href="/wiki/Last_judgement" class="mw-redirect" title="Last judgement">last judgement</a> was of profound concern to him." \xe2\x80\x93 (Engelmann)</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Wittgenstein&amp;rft.pages=218&amp;rft.pub=Taylor+%26+Francis&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.isbn=9781136731372&amp;rft.au=William+Child&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALudwig+Wittgenstein" Toronto: University of Toronto Press, 2015.</li>\n<li>Xanthos, Nicolas, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.signosemio.com/wittgenstein/language-games.asp">"Wittgenstein\'s Language Games"</a>, in Louis Hebert (dir.), <i>Signo</i> (online), Rimouski (Quebec, Canada), 2006.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Works_referencing_Wittgenstein">Works referencing Wittgenstein</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ludwig_Wittgenstein&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Works referencing Wittgenstein">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/E._L._Doctorow" title="E. L. Doctorow">Doctorow, E. L.</a>  <i>City of God</i>. Plume, 2001, depicts an imaginary rivalry between Wittgenstein and Einstein.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Apostolos_Doxiadis" title="Apostolos Doxiadis">Doxiadis, Apostolos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christos_Papadimitriou" title="Christos Papadimitriou">Papadimitriou, Christos</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Logicomix" title="Logicomix">Logicomix</a></i>. Bloomsbury, 2009.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bruce_Duffy" title="Bruce Duffy">Duffy, Bruce</a>. <i>The World as I Found It</i>. Ticknor &amp; Fields, 1987, a recreation of Wittgenstein\'s life.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Derek_Jarman" title="Derek Jarman">Jarman, Derek</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Wittgenstein_(film)" title="Wittgenstein (film)">Wittgenstein</a></i>, a biopic of Wittgenstein with a script by <a href="/wiki/Terry_Eagleton" title="Terry Eagleton">Terry Eagleton</a>, British Film Institute, 1993.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Philip_Kerr" title="Philip Kerr">Kerr, Philip</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/A_Philosophical_Investigation" title="A Philosophical Investigation">A Philosophical Investigation</a></i>, Chatto &amp; Windus, 1992, a dystopian thriller set in 2012.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/David_Markson" title="David Markson">Markson, David</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Wittgenstein%27s_Mistress" title="Wittgenstein&#39;s Mistress">Wittgenstein\'s Mistress</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Dalkey_Archive_Press" title="Dalkey Archive Press">Dalkey Archive Press</a>, 1988, an experimental novel, a first-person account of what it would be like to live in the world of the <i>Tractatus</i>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/James_Tully_(philosopher)" title="James Tully (philosopher)">Tully, James</a>. <i>Strange Multiplicity: Constitutionalism in an Age of Diversity</i>. Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1995</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/David_Foster_Wallace" title="David Foster Wallace">Wallace, David Foster</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Broom_of_the_System" title="The Broom of the System">The Broom of the System</a></i>. Penguin Books, 1987, a novel.</li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_links">External links</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
